## Questions and answers

### Q001. What is the definition of a "large vehicle" parking space?

**Reference answer:** "Large vehicle" means the minimum size of a large vehicle parking space shall be 8 feet in width and 19 feet in length

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> A "large vehicle" parking space is defined as having a minimum width of 8 feet and a minimum length of 19 feet. `SMC 23.54.030.A.1`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> A “large vehicle” parking space in Seattle is defined in SMC 23.54.030 as having a minimum size of 8 feet in width and 19 feet in length.[Source 1] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.829

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 2/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> In Seattle, a "large vehicle" parking space is defined in the zoning code rather than the building code. --- # Definition Under the Seattle Municipal Code Chapter 23.54: > A large vehicle parking space is a parking stall sized to accommodate vehicles larger than a standard passenger car—such as vans, trucks, or similar oversized vehicles. --- # Required dimensions The code specifies minimum dimensions: * Width: typically ≥ 8.5–9 feet (same or slightly wider than standard stalls depending on configuration) * Length: ≥ 19–23 feet (longer than standard 18 ft stalls) 👉 The key distinction is length, not just width. --- # When large vehicle spaces are required Seattle requires a portion of parking to be sized for large vehicles: * In many uses, at least 10% of parking spaces must qualify as large vehicle spaces * These are intended to accommodate: * Pickup trucks * Vans * SUVs (especially larger models) --- # Practical interpretation in design In real projects: * Standard stall: ~8.5' × 18' * Large vehicle stall: ~8.5–9' × 20–23' Designers often: * Place them at the ends of aisles or along walls * Use them to handle overhang constraints --- # Important nuance * This is a zoning requirement (SMC Title 23) * It is separate from ADA/accessible parking requirements * It also differs from loading berths, which are much larger and serve delivery vehicles --- # Bottom line A large vehicle parking space in Seattle is simply: > A longer-than-standard parking stall (generally 20–23 feet deep) intended to accommodate oversized passenger vehicles, with a required percentage of such spaces in many developments. --- If you want, I can pull the exact percentage and dimensional table for your specific use (multifamily vs commercial)—it varies slightly and can affect your garage layout efficiency.

---

### Q002. What is the definition of "tandem parking"

**Reference answer:** "Tandem parking" means a parking space equal to the width and two times the length of the vehicle size standards in subsections 23.54.030.A.1, 23.54.030.A.2, and 23.54.030.A.3 for the size of the vehicle to be accommodated

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 0/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> Not found in provided sources.

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 1/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> This could not be determined from the available materials; no Washington State (RCW/WAC/IBC_WA) or Seattle (SMC/DIR/SPU) definitions of “tandem parking” were retrieved in the context above. In practice, many jurisdictions use “tandem parking” to mean two (or more) parking spaces arranged one behind the other so that one vehicle must be moved to access the other, but I cannot confirm the exact legally controlling definition for Washington State or Seattle from the provided sources. To get the binding definition, you would need to check: - The specific Seattle Municipal Code chapter on land use/parking (often SMC 23.54 or similar), and - Any related Seattle Director’s Rules defining parking space types.

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 2/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> In Seattle, "tandem parking" is defined in the zoning code. --- # Definition Under the Seattle Municipal Code Chapter 23.54: > Tandem parking means two or more parking spaces arranged one behind another in a single line, such that one vehicle must be moved to allow another vehicle to enter or exit. --- # Key characteristics * Spaces are aligned end-to-end (not side-by-side) * They typically share a single access point/drive aisle * The "inner" vehicle is blocked in by the "outer" vehicle --- # How it's treated in Seattle * Often allowed in residential uses, especially: * Single-family / Neighborhood Residential * Some multifamily contexts (with conditions) * Typically restricted or discouraged for: * Commercial or public parking * Situations where independent access is required --- # Practical example Imagine a driveway or garage: ``` [ Drive aisle ] ↓ [ Car A ] ← blocks [ Car B ] ← blocked ``` * Car B cannot exit unless Car A moves → this is tandem parking. --- # Why it matters in design * Efficiency: increases parking count in tight sites * Usability trade-off: less convenient for users * Code compliance: may or may not count toward required parking depending on use --- # Bottom line > Tandem parking = stacked parking spaces in a line with dependent access, where vehicles cannot independently enter or exit. --- If you want, I can explain when tandem spaces count toward required parking in Seattle multifamily projects—that's where developers often run into surprises.

### Q003. What are the specific dimensions required for an off-street bus parking space?

**Reference answer:** Bus parking spaces, when required, shall be 13 feet in width and 40 feet in length

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> An off-street bus parking space, when required, must be at least 13 feet wide and 40 feet long. `SMC 23.49.019.I`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 0/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> The retrieved IBC_WA provisions specify dimensions for *accessible car and van parking spaces* and access aisles, but do not provide any dimensions specifically for off-street bus parking spaces. They require car and van parking spaces to be at least 96 inches (2440 mm) wide, with access aisles 60 inches (1525 mm) wide for cars and 96 inches (2440 mm) wide for vans, and signage mounted at least 60 inches (1525 mm) above the parking surface.[Source 3] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § IBC International Building Code, p.144; [Source 2] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § IBC International Building Code, p.144. | Space Type | Minimum Width | Citation | |-----------------------------------|---------------|----------| | Car and van accessible space | 96 in (2440 mm) | [Source 3] | | Access aisle (car space) | 60 in (1525 mm) | [Source 3] | | Access aisle (van space) | 96 in (2440 mm) | [Source 3] | Specific dimensional standards for off-street bus parking spaces could not be determined from the available IBC_WA materials.

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 0/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> In Seattle, the required dimensions for an off-street bus parking (loading) space are specified in the zoning/loading standards. --- # Definition and dimensions Under the Seattle Municipal Code Chapter 23.54, a bus parking space (often treated as a large loading berth) must be at least: * Width: 12 feet * Length: 60 feet * Vertical clearance: 14 feet --- # How it's classified Seattle does not always use the term "bus parking" as a standalone category. Instead, these spaces are typically regulated as: * Large loading berths * Designed to accommodate: * Buses * Large trucks * Tour vehicles --- # Design implications * Requires significant maneuvering space (turning radii often exceed 50–60 ft) * Usually located: * At-grade * Along alleys or service drives * Rarely feasible in structured garages due to: * Clearance constraints * Turning limitations --- # Important nuance * These requirements are zoning-driven (SMC Title 23) * Separate from: * Standard parking stalls * ADA spaces * May be triggered by specific uses (e.g., hotels, large assembly uses, institutional buildings) --- # Bottom line > A Seattle off-street bus parking space is effectively a large loading berth sized at about 12′ × 60′ with 14′ vertical clearance, designed to accommodate buses and similar large vehicles. --- If you want, I can walk through which Seattle land uses actually trigger a required bus/loading berth—that's often where projects either overbuild or miss a requirement.

### Q004. What is the maximum permitted slope for a driveway?

**Reference answer:** No portion of a driveway, whether located on a lot or on a right-of-way, shall exceed a slope of 15 percent, except as provided in this subsection 23.54.030.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> The maximum permitted slope for a driveway is 15 percent, unless the Director permits a steeper slope when a 15 percent maximum is infeasible due to topography or other special lot characteristics, the additional slope is the least amount necessary, and the driveway remains usable as access to the lot. `[SMC 23.54.030]`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 1/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> From the available materials, the only explicit driveway slope-related standard is for fire apparatus access roads, not general private driveways. For fire apparatus access roads, the gradient must be “negotiable by the specific fire apparatus normally used at that location,” but no specific maximum slope (e.g., percent grade) is stated in the retrieved text; it instead requires that the road be all-weather, at least 20 feet wide, 13 feet 6 inches clear height, and designed for fire apparatus loads and turning radii. [Source 2] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § International Wildland Urban Interface Code 403.3, p.17. The maximum slope for ordinary driveways serving buildings could not be determined from the available materials.

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 1/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> In Seattle, driveway slope limits come from both zoning and right-of-way standards, but the controlling provision for most projects is in the Land Use Code. --- # Maximum driveway slope (on private property) Under the Seattle Municipal Code Chapter 23.54: > The maximum permitted slope for a driveway is generally 20%. --- # Additional constraints (important in practice) ## 1. Transition areas Even though 20% is allowed, the code requires: * Flatter transitions at: * The sidewalk / right-of-way * Garage entry 👉 This prevents vehicles from: * Bottoming out * Blocking sidewalks --- ## 2. Right-of-way portion (often stricter) Within the public right-of-way (regulated separately through SDOT): * Slopes are typically limited to much lower grades (~10–15%) * Must comply with: * Sidewalk cross-slope rules * ADA accessibility requirements 👉 This is often the real constraint, not the 20% private limit. --- ## 3. Practical design limit Even though 20% is legally allowed: * Most designers aim for 12–15% max for usability * Steeper slopes create: * Safety issues (especially in wet/icy conditions) * Problems for low-clearance vehicles --- # Where conflicts arise You'll often see tension between: * Topography (steep Seattle sites) * Garage elevation (especially below grade) * Right-of-way constraints 👉 Result: > You may be forced to redesign garage depth, entry location, or use ramps inside the building. --- # Bottom line * Maximum allowed (private property): ~20% slope * Practical/ROW-limited reality: often lower due to SDOT + ADA constraints --- If you want, I can sketch a typical Seattle steep-site driveway profile showing exactly where projects fail slope compliance—that's a very common permitting issue.

### Q005. Are you allowed to park a vehicle on a planting strip?

**Reference answer:** Parking on planting strips is prohibited.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> You are not allowed to stop, stand, or park a vehicle on a planting strip unless the vehicle bears a special placard or license plate issued under RCW Chapter 46.19 (disability parking). `[SMC 11.72.320]`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> No, parking a vehicle on a planting strip is prohibited by the Seattle Municipal Code, which states: “Parking on planting strips is prohibited.” [Source 3] SMC — smc_chapters, § 23.44.016, p.170. Separately, the traffic code also bars stopping, standing, or parking a vehicle on a “planting strip” unless the vehicle has a special disabled placard or license plate issued under RCW Chapter 46.19. [Source 1] SMC — smc_chapters, § 11.72.320, p.284.

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> In Seattle, you generally are NOT allowed to park a vehicle on a planting strip (also called a "parking strip" or "landscaped strip"). ### What is a planting strip? It's the area between the sidewalk and the curb—often landscaped with grass, trees, or shrubs. Even though it's next to your property, it is typically part of the public right-of-way, not private parking space. ### The rule Under Seattle municipal code: * Parking is prohibited on planting strips. * This applies to cars, trucks, trailers, and RVs. * The rule exists to: * Protect street trees and utilities * Prevent soil compaction and drainage issues * Maintain pedestrian safety and accessibility ### Exceptions (rare) * If there is a formally constructed driveway or curb cut approved by the city. * Temporary construction uses with permits. ### Enforcement & consequences * You can receive a parking ticket. * In some cases, the vehicle may be towed. * Damage to trees or infrastructure can result in additional fines or restoration costs. ### Practical takeaway If it's not a paved, permitted parking area or driveway, assume you cannot park there—even if it's directly in front of your house. If you want, I can point you to the exact Seattle code section or help you check a specific property situation (some edge cases do come up).

### Q006. Is off-street motor vehicle parking required when building an accessory dwelling unit?

**Reference answer:** No parking is required for accessory dwelling units.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> No. Seattle’s ADU regulations state that no off-street motor vehicle parking is required for an accessory dwelling unit. `SMC 23.42.022.I`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> Off-street motor vehicle parking is not required for accessory dwelling units (ADUs) in Seattle, and state law generally prohibits cities from imposing off‑street parking requirements for ADUs near major transit, with limited safety-based exceptions. 1. Brief Issue Overview RCW 36.70A restricts cities from requiring off-street parking for ADUs within ¼–½ mile of major transit stops, unless the city meets specific evidentiary and certification conditions to show safety concerns, or is within 1 mile of a large commercial airport.[Source 1][Source 2][Source 3] Separately, Seattle’s Land Use Code goes further and states flatly that “no off-street motor vehicle parking is required for an accessory dwelling unit,” without limiting this to transit areas.[Source 11][Source 14] Because RCW is higher authority, Seattle cannot impose stricter ADU parking requirements than allowed by state law, but it is free to be more permissive, which it has done. There is therefore no direct conflict: state law sets a floor of reduced requirements, and Seattle eliminates minimum parking for ADUs entirely. 2. Side-by-Side Comparison | Topic | Agency | Requirement | Citation | |-------|--------|-------------|----------| | ADU off-street parking near transit (¼ mile) | RCW | Cities may not require off-street parking for ADUs within ¼ mile of a major transit stop, unless they determine there is lack of street parking capacity, physical impediments, or other evidence-based reasons making on-street parking infeasible. | [Source 1] | | ADU off-street parking near transit (½ mile) | RCW | Cities planning under RCW 36.70A.040 may not require off-street parking for ADUs within ½ mile walking distance of a major transit stop, and may not require more than 1–2 spaces per ADU depending on lot size, unless they obtain Commerce certification via an empirical safety study or fall within 1 mile of a large commercial airport. | [Source 2][Source 3] | | General ADU off-street parking in Seattle | SMC | “No off-street motor vehicle parking is required for an accessory dwelling unit.” | [Source 11] | | Minimum parking table – ADUs | SMC | Table B for 23.54.015 reiterates: “No parking is required for accessory dwelling units.” | [Source 14] | 4. Friction Summary | # | Agencies | Friction Type | Winner | Risk Note | |---|----------|---------------|--------|-----------| | 1 | RCW vs SMC | Type II — Gap/Silence (state sets limits; city more permissive) | RCW sets floor; SMC is consistent and more lenient | Seattle’s “no parking required” rule for ADUs is more permissive than state minimums and does not appear to be preempted. | 5. Key Takeaways - In Seattle, you are not required to provide any off-street motor vehicle parking when building an ADU.[Source 11][Source 14] - State law independently bars cities from imposing off-street parking requirements for ADUs near major transit, and caps how much parking can be required elsewhere, unless strict safety-study exceptions are met.[Source 1][Source 2][Source 3] - You may still choose to build parking voluntarily, but it is not a code-required condition for permitting an ADU in Seattle under the cited provisions.

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 2/5

> In Seattle, off-street motor vehicle parking is NOT required when building an accessory dwelling unit (ADU) under current rules. ### The key rule (today) * Seattle has eliminated minimum off-street parking requirements for ADUs. ([CityRuleLookup][1]) * This applies to both: * Attached ADUs (AADUs) * Detached ADUs (DADUs / backyard cottages) ### Important nuance (this trips people up) Even though you don't have to add parking, there is still a constraint: * You generally cannot remove existing required off-street parking for the main house unless you replace it elsewhere on the property. ([City of Seattle][2]) So: * ✅ No new parking required for the ADU itself * ⚠️ But you can't eliminate required parking tied to the primary dwelling without replacement ### Why this matters in practice This creates a subtle design constraint: * If your lot already has no required parking → you're free (no parking obligation at all) * If your lot has required parking (e.g., a garage counted as a required space) → converting it to an ADU may trigger a need to reprovide that space elsewhere ### Big-picture context Seattle removed ADU parking minimums as part of broader housing reforms to: * Increase housing supply * Reduce development barriers * Support transit-oriented living --- ### Bottom line You do not need to provide off-street parking for a new ADU in Seattle, but you must be careful not to eliminate any existing required parking without replacing it. --- If you want, I can walk through a specific lot scenario (e.g., garage conversion vs. backyard DADU) — that's where the edge cases really show up. [1]: https://www.cityrulelookup.com/accessory-structures/adu-rules/seattle-wa?utm_source=chatgpt.com "Seattle, WA Accessory Structures: ADU Rules Rules (2026) | CityRuleLookup" [2]: https://www.cityofseattle.org/sdci/permits/common-projects/accessory-dwelling-units?utm_source=chatgpt.com "Accessory Dwelling Unit - SDCI | seattle.gov"

### Q007. What happens to temporary parking facilities that were provided for construction workers after the construction is finished?

**Reference answer:** Temporary parking provided for construction workers must be terminated or removed when construction is completed.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> Temporary parking facilities provided for construction workers must be terminated or removed when construction is completed. `SMC 23.42.044.C`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> Temporary parking facilities for construction workers in Seattle are explicitly treated as short-term and must be removed or converted when the project is done. SMC states that temporary parking facilities provided for construction workers are exempt from the normal parking requirements of the underlying zone and SMC 23.54 only while they are temporary, and that such temporary parking “must be terminated or removed when construction is completed.” [Source 9] SMC also authorizes the Director, when reviewing permits, to require that construction-worker parking be located on the construction site as a mitigation measure, reinforcing that this is a temporary, construction-phase condition rather than a permanent parking entitlement. [Source 12] | Topic | Agency | Requirement | Citation | |-------|--------|-------------|----------| | Status of construction-worker parking after construction | SMC | Temporary parking facilities for construction workers are exempt from normal parking requirements but must be terminated or removed when construction is completed. | [Source 9] | | Construction-related parking mitigation | SMC | Director may require construction-worker parking on the construction site as mitigation for construction impacts, indicating it is tied to the construction period only. | [Source 12] |

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 2/5

> In Seattle, temporary parking facilities provided for construction workers are not allowed to remain as permanent parking once construction is complete—they must be removed or converted into a code-compliant use. ### What typically happens after construction ends 1. They must be removed * Temporary gravel lots, staging areas, or leased off-site parking used during construction are only permitted for the construction period. * Once the project is complete (or the permit expires), these areas are expected to be cleared and restored. 2. The site must be restored or built out per permit * The area may need to be: * Returned to landscaping or open space * Built out as approved permanent parking (if included in the final permit) * Restored to its original condition if it was a temporary disturbance 3. They cannot "quietly become permanent" * If a developer wants to keep parking: * It must meet zoning, land use, and stormwater requirements * It usually requires a separate permit and review * Temporary lots (e.g., gravel) typically don't meet permanent parking standards (paving, drainage, striping, etc.) 4. Enforcement * The city (through SDCI / SDOT) can: * Withhold final sign-off (certificate of occupancy) * Require restoration as a condition of permit closure * Issue violations if temporary uses remain --- ### Why the city is strict about this Temporary parking can: * Conflict with zoning (especially in residential zones) * Create drainage and stormwater issues * Occupy space intended for required landscaping or setbacks --- ### Practical takeaway Think of construction worker parking as a temporary entitlement tied to the construction permit—once the project ends, that entitlement disappears unless you've explicitly permitted something permanent. --- If you're planning a project, I can help you spot where temporary staging/parking plans might accidentally create compliance risks at close-out—that's a common (and costly) oversight.

### Q008. What is the procedure for determining the required amount of off-street parking if a specific use is not listed on the standard parking tables?

**Reference answer:** In the case of a use not shown on Table A, Table B, or Table C, the requirements for off-street parking will be determined by the Director based on the requirements for the most comparable use

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> If a proposed use is not listed on Tables A, B, or C of `SMC 23.54.015`, the Director determines the required off‑street parking as follows: - The Director must base the requirement on the most comparable listed use when, in their judgment, a comparable use exists. `[SMC 23.54.015.H]` - If the Director finds that none of the listed uses are comparable, they may set the requirement based on detailed information provided by the applicant, which can include a description of the physical structure, identification of potential users, and an analysis of likely parking demand. `[SMC 23.54.015.H]`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> If a proposed use is not listed on Tables A, B, or C for SMC 23.54.015, the Director determines the off‑street parking requirement based on the requirements for the most comparable listed use.[Source 2] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.791 If, in the Director’s judgment, none of the listed uses are comparable, the Director may instead set the requirement based on detailed information provided by the applicant, which can include a description of the physical structures, identification of potential users, and an analysis of likely parking demand.[Source 4] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.792

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 2/5

> In Seattle, when a specific land use is not listed in the standard off-street parking tables, the city doesn't leave it undefined—it uses an administrative determination process. ### How the procedure works 1. Director's determination (SDCI) * The Seattle Department of Construction and Inspections Director (or designee) determines the required parking. * They assign the unlisted use to a comparable listed use in the code. 2. "Most similar use" analysis The Director evaluates: * The function and activity of the proposed use * Expected number of employees, customers, or residents * Trip generation and parking demand characteristics * Operational patterns (hours, turnover, etc.) Then they match it to the closest category (e.g., treating a new hybrid retail concept like a restaurant or general retail, depending on how it operates). 3. Apply the corresponding standard * Once matched, the parking ratio for that comparable use is applied. * Any applicable reductions or overlays (e.g., frequent transit areas, urban centers) still apply. 4. Possible modifications or flexibility * The Director may: * Require more or less parking if justified by evidence * Consider shared parking, transportation demand management (TDM), or site constraints * In some cases, a formal interpretation or adjustment may be issued. --- ### What this means in practice * You're not stuck just because your use is "new" or hybrid. * But you also don't get to choose the category yourself—the city will decide based on how your use actually functions. * Early coordination with SDCI is key, especially for: * Mixed-use concepts * New business models (e.g., co-working + retail + event space) --- ### Practical tip Before submitting for permits, many developers request a pre-submittal or land use interpretation from SDCI to lock in the parking classification—this avoids redesign later. --- If you want, tell me your specific use (even if it's unconventional), and I can walk through how Seattle would likely classify it and what parking requirement you'd end up with.

### Q009. What are the specific provisions and requirements for permitting parking and driveways as a conditional use accessory to a solid waste transfer station in a C2 zone?

**Reference answer:** Parking and driveways on property in a C2 zone are permitted as a conditional use accessory to a solid waste transfer station according to the following provisions: The parking is on property that is part of the same development site as the solid waste transfer station use; The parking meets the criteria of Section 23.50.014.A; The parking is subject to analysis in a transportation plan, if required by the Director; Surface parking is not across the street from a residentially zoned lot; A setback of at least 65 feet is required between surface parking spaces and any lot line that abuts or is across a street from a commercially zoned lot; and Driveways providing access to parking or access to the solid waste transfer station are on the same development site as the solid waste transfer station use.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> - Parking and driveways on property in a C2 zone may be permitted as a conditional use accessory to a solid waste transfer station only if all of the following are met: - The parking is on property that is part of the same development site as the solid waste transfer station use. `SMC 23.46.022.A.8.a` - The parking meets the criteria of subsection `23.50.014.A` (general conditional use criteria for industrial zones). `SMC 23.46.022.A.8.b` - The parking is included in and subject to analysis in a transportation plan, if the Director requires such a plan under `23.50.014.B.7.c`. `SMC 23.46.022.A.8.c` - Additional siting and separation standards for such accessory parking/driveways in C2: - Surface parking is not allowed across the street from a residentially zoned lot. `SMC 23.46.022.A.8.d` - A minimum setback of 65 feet is required between surface parking spaces and any lot line that abuts or is across a street from a commercially zoned lot. `SMC 23.46.022.A.8.e` - Driveways providing access to the parking or to the solid waste transfer station must be on the same development site as the solid waste transfer station use. `SMC 23.46.022.A.8.f`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> The IBC sets statewide building requirements for accessible EV charging and waste storage, while Seattle’s Land Use Code (SMC) and a Director’s Rule govern where parking and driveways for solid waste transfer stations can be located and under what conditions. For a C2-zoned site, the key issue is that parking and driveways accessory to a solid waste transfer station are allowed only as a conditional use and must meet specific siting, setback, and impact-mitigation criteria in the SMC; no IBC_WA or DIR provisions change those land use conditions, but DIR further restricts crossing into single-family zones. [Source 1] [Source 8] [Source 24] --- ### 1. Side-by-Side Comparison | Topic | Agency | Requirement | Citation | |-------|--------|-------------|----------| | EV charging at accessible spaces (general building requirement) | IBC_WA | 10% of accessible parking spaces must be EV charging stations and an additional 10% must be EV-ready; up to 10% of accessible spaces may count toward total EV spaces required. Applies to all new buildings, including those with parking, but does not itself authorize land use or conditional use of parking. | [Source 1] | | Solid waste / recycling storage (general building requirement) | IBC_WA | All new buildings must provide space for recyclable materials, compost, and solid waste storage (except Group R-3 and U); this is independent of zoning/conditional use status. | [Source 1] | | Parking & driveways accessory to solid waste transfer station in C2 zone – conditional use status | SMC | Parking and driveways on property in a C2 zone are permitted only as a conditional use when accessory to a solid waste transfer station on the same development site. | [Source 8] | | C2 recycling use on same site as transfer station – related conditions | SMC | A recycling use on the same development site as a solid waste transfer station in a C2 zone may be permitted as a conditional use; conditions include: 65-foot setback between any façade of the principal recycling structure and any lot line abutting or across a street from a residentially zoned lot; trucks dropping recyclables limited to a maximum of two axles; all transfer/handling/compacting of recyclables must be within an enclosed structure; outdoor storage prohibited; 60% of the C2-zoned portion of the site must be maintained as open space (surface parking and driveways are not counted as open space); office and crew space accessory to the transfer station may be allowed within the recycling structure. These conditions strongly constrain where accessory parking/driveways can be placed on the C2 portion. | [Source 4] [Source 8] | | C2 accessory parking & driveways – same development site requirement | SMC | Parking and driveways accessory to a solid waste transfer station in a C2 zone must be located on property that is part of the same development site as the solid waste transfer station use. | parking – separation from residential zones | SMC | Surface parking accessory to a solid waste transfer station in a C2 zone must not be located across the street from a residentially zoned lot. | [Source 2] | | C2 accessory parking – setback from commercial lots | SMC | A minimum 65-foot setback is required between surface parking spaces and any lot line that abuts or is across a street from a commercially zoned lot, for parking accessory to a solid waste transfer station in a C2 zone. | [Source 2] | | C2 accessory parking – access location | SMC | Driveways providing access to parking or to the solid waste transfer station must be located on the same development site as the solid waste transfer station use. | [Source 2] [Source 8] | | Access across zones to solid waste transfer station | SMC | Vehicular and pedestrian access to a solid waste transfer station use may cross property in an Industrial or C2 zone, even if the station itself is in another zone, but such access is subject to the conditional use criteria applicable to the principal solid waste transfer station use. | [Source 12] | | Access/parking of multifamily structures on single-family zoned portion | DIR | For lots zoned partly single-family and partly multifamily, no portion of a multifamily structure, its parking, access, or accessory structures may be located in the single-family zoned portion; this limits routing of transfer-station-related parking/access through single-family areas even if part of a larger lot. | [Source 24] | | Industrial/IB/UI/IC zones – accessory parking & landscaping (context) | SMC | In IB, IC, and UI zones, parking and driveways accessory to a solid waste transfer station may be permitted as conditional uses with similar “same development site” and transportation-plan requirements; a 20-foot landscaped area is required between any structure or parking and the nearest street lot line, and scale houses are limited to 1,000 sq ft. These illustrate parallel but zone-specific standards that do not directly change C2 rules but show typical conditions the City imposes. | [Source 7] [Source 19] | --- ### 2. Friction Summary | # | Agencies | Friction Type | Winner | Risk Note | |---|----------|---------------|--------|-----------| | 1 | IBC_WA vs SMC | Type II — Gap/Silence (different subject matter) | SMC for land use; IBC_WA for building design | IBC_WA governs EV charging and waste-storage design inside the project, while SMC controls whether and where accessory parking/driveways are allowed in C2; both must be satisfied. | | 2 | SMC vs DIR | Type II — Gap/Silence (different parcels/zones) | SMC for C2; DIR for mixed SF/MF lots | DIR 9-2002 does not alter C2 conditional use standards but adds a separate constraint if any part of the lot is single-family zoned, limiting where access/parking can be placed. | --- ### 3. Key Takeaways - In a C2 zone, parking and driveways serving a solid waste transfer station are allowed only as a conditional use, must be on the same development site as the transfer station, and must not place surface parking across the street from residentially zoned lots. [Source 2] [Source 8] - Surface parking must be set back at least 65 feet from any lot line that abuts or is across a street from a commercially zoned lot, and any required transportation plan must analyze the accessory parking and driveways. [Source 2] - If the C2 site also includes a recycling use or abuts other zones, additional conditions (open space minimums, enclosure of operations, truck size limits, and access across zones) will further constrain the layout and location of accessory parking and driveways. [Source 4] [Source 12]

> In Seattle, the Seattle Municipal Code lays out very specific conditional-use criteria for allowing parking and driveways accessory to a solid waste transfer station in a C2 (Commercial 2) zone. These are not automatic—they require meeting multiple layered conditions under SMC 23.47A.006. Here's a clear breakdown of the requirements: --- # 1) Must be part of the same development site * The parking cannot be on a separate parcel. * It must be physically part of the same development site as the solid waste transfer station. 👉 This prevents "remote" parking lots serving industrial uses in unrelated locations. --- # 2) Must comply with general parking standards (SMC 23.50.014.A) * The parking must meet baseline citywide parking design and operational standards, including: * Layout and access * Screening, landscaping, and location controls * Safety and circulation 👉 In other words, this is not a waiver of normal parking rules—it's an added layer on top. --- # 3) Subject to transportation plan analysis (if required) * If the Seattle Department of Construction and Inspections Director requires a transportation plan, then: * The parking and driveways must be analyzed as part of that plan * Impacts like traffic, truck circulation, and access must be evaluated 👉 This is especially important because transfer stations generate heavy truck traffic. --- # 4) Restrictions relative to residential zones * Surface parking is prohibited across the street from residentially zoned lots. 👉 This is a strong buffering rule to protect nearby residential areas from: * Noise * Visual impacts * Truck activity --- # 5) Setback requirement (commercial adjacency) * A minimum 65-foot setback is required: * Between surface parking areas and * Any lot line that abuts or is across a street from a commercially zoned lot 👉 This is unusually large and reflects the intensity of the use. --- # 6) Driveways must also be on-site * Any driveways providing access to: * Parking, or * The transfer station itself * Must be located on the same development site as the transfer station 👉 Again, this avoids off-site circulation impacts. --- # 7) Conditional use approval framework still applies Even if all the above are met, approval is not automatic: * The proposal must still satisfy general conditional use criteria (SMC 23.42.042), such as: * Compatibility with surrounding uses * Adequate infrastructure * Mitigation of impacts (noise, traffic, aesthetics) --- # Key insight (what makes this unusual) This provision is highly restrictive compared to typical accessory parking because: * It ties parking strictly to the same site * It imposes large setbacks and location bans * It requires transportation-level analysis 👉 The city is effectively saying: > "We'll allow this, but only if its impacts are tightly controlled and contained." --- # Practical takeaway If you're planning this type of facility: * You should expect: * Site-constrained design (can't rely on nearby parcels) * Significant buffering and setbacks * Traffic engineering involvement early in design * Early coordination with SDCI is critical, because interpretation of: * "same development site" * transportation plan triggers can materially affect feasibility. --- If you want, I can map these requirements onto a hypothetical site layout (e.g., where parking could realistically go on a constrained C2 parcel).

### Q010. What are all the conditions that must be met to establish parking accessory to a single-family structure existing on June 11, 1982, on another lot?

**Reference answer:** Parking accessory to a single-family structure existing on June 11, 1982, may be established on another lot if all the following conditions are met: There is no vehicular access to permissible parking areas on the lot; Any garage constructed is for no more than two two-axle, or two up to four-wheeled vehicles; Parking is screened or landscaped as required by the Director; The lot providing the parking is within the same block or across the alley from the principal use lot; and The accessory parking shall be tied to the lot of the principal use by a covenant or other document recorded with the King County Recorder's Office.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> - The parking must be accessory to a single-family structure existing on June 11, 1982. `SMC 23.44.016.C.5` - There must be no vehicular access to permissible parking areas on the lot with the single-family structure. `SMC 23.44.016.C.5.a` - Any garage constructed on the other lot must be for no more than two two-axle, or two up to four‑wheeled vehicles. `SMC 23.44.016.C.5.b` - The parking must be screened or landscaped as required by the Director, who shall consider development patterns of the block or nearby blocks. `SMC 23.44.016.C.5.c` - The lot providing the parking must be within the same block or across the alley from the principal use lot. `SMC 23.44.016.C.5.d` - The accessory parking must be tied to the lot of the principal use by a covenant or other document recorded with the King County Recorder's Office. `SMC 23.44.016.C.5.e`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> Parking for a single-family structure that existed on June 11, 1982 may be located on another lot only if all of the following conditions are met under SMC 23.44.016: Side-by-Side Comparison | Topic | Agency | Requirement | Citation | |-------|--------|-------------|----------| | Eligibility date | SMC | The parking must be accessory to a single-family structure existing on June 11, 1982. | [Source 2] | | No on-site access | SMC | There is no vehicular access to permissible parking areas on the lot with the single-family structure. | [Source 2] | | Garage size limit | SMC | Any garage constructed on the other lot is for no more than two two-axle, or two up to four‑wheeled vehicles. | [Source 4] | | Screening/landscaping | SMC | Parking on the other lot must be screened or landscaped as required by the Director, who considers development patterns of the block or nearby blocks. | [Source 4] | | Proximity of lots | SMC | The lot providing the parking must be within the same block or across the alley from the principal-use lot. | [Source 4] | | Recorded covenant | SMC | The accessory parking must be tied to the lot of the principal use by a covenant or other recorded document with the King County Recorder’s Office. | [Source 4] | Key Takeaways - All six conditions above must be satisfied; if any one is not met, off-lot accessory parking under this provision is not allowed. - The relationship between the parking lot and the dwelling lot must be formalized by a recorded covenant linking the parking use to the principal structure.

> In Seattle, the ability to establish accessory off-site parking for a single-family structure that existed on June 11, 1982 is a very narrow, grandfather-type provision in the Land Use Code. It's allowed only if all conditions are met. Here are the full set of requirements, distilled clearly: --- # Core concept This provision allows a single-family home (existing as of June 11, 1982) to locate its required parking on a separate lot, but only under strict controls to prevent abuse. --- # Required conditions ### 1) The house must predate June 11, 1982 * The principal structure must be a single-family dwelling that legally existed on that date 👉 This is a nonconforming relief provision, not available to newer homes --- ### 2) Parking must be accessory to that specific dwelling * The off-site parking must serve only that house * It cannot be: * Shared broadly * Used for other uses or commercial purposes --- ### 3) Location must be nearby and within limits * The parking must be located: * On another lot, but * Within a limited distance (typically within the same vicinity/block—interpreted narrowly by the City) 👉 The intent is to keep the parking functionally tied to the residence --- ### 4) Zoning of the parking lot must allow it * The lot used for parking must be in a zone where: * Accessory parking is allowed or conditionally allowed * You cannot place it in a zone where parking would otherwise be prohibited --- ### This is critical—the City wants to ensure the parking cannot be separated later --- ### 6) Parking must comply with development standards Even though it's accessory, the parking must still meet: * Parking design standards (layout, dimensions, access) * Landscaping / screening requirements * Driveway and access rules --- ### 7) No creation of new nonconformities * The off-site parking arrangement: * Cannot increase nonconformity * Cannot violate setbacks, coverage, or use rules on either lot --- ### 8) Quantity is limited to what is required * You can only provide: * The required number of spaces (or a limited accessory amount) * You cannot create a large off-site parking lot under this provision --- ### 9) Continued use requirement * If the parking: * Is removed, or * Stops serving the dwelling → The entitlement may be lost, and re-establishing it may not be allowed --- # Why this exists This rule exists because many older Seattle homes: * Were built before modern parking requirements * Sit on lots where parking cannot physically be added So the City allows a workaround—but tightly controlled. --- # Practical takeaway This is a grandfathered, exception-based pathway, not a flexible tool: * It works best for: * Older homes with no on-site parking options * It becomes risky if: * Ownership changes * The legal agreement isn't properly recorded * The off-site lot is redeveloped --- If you want, I can walk through a real scenario (e.g., using a neighboring lot vs. across an alley) and show where this provision works—and where it typically fails in ### Q011. In multifamily zones, where is surface parking permitted to be located on a lot, and what areas are specifically excluded?

**Reference answer:** Except as otherwise provided, surface parking may be located anywhere on a lot except: in the required front setback; in the required side street side setback as extended from side lot line to side lot line; and within 20 feet of any street lot line. However, if access is taken directly from an alley, surface parking may be located anywhere within 28 feet from an alley lot line provided it is no closer than 7 feet to any street lot line, and for lots with only alley frontage, surface parking may be located within the front setback.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> In multifamily zones, surface parking location is governed by `SMC 23.45.536`: - Surface parking may be located anywhere on a lot except in the following areas: - Required front setback `[SMC 23.45.536.B.2.a.1]` - Required side street side setback (as extended from side lot line to side lot line) `[SMC 23.45.536.B.2.a.2]` - Within 20 feet of any street lot line `[SMC 23.45.536.B.2.a.3]` - If access is taken directly from an alley, surface parking may be located anywhere within 28 feet of an alley lot line, provided it is no closer than 7 feet to any street lot line; for lots with only alley frontage, surface parking may be located within the front setback `[SMC 23.45.536.B.2.b]`.

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 1/5, Faithfulness 2/5

> In multifamily zones, accessory surface parking must generally be located at the rear or to the side of the principal structure, and the lot area devoted to accessory surface parking is limited to 30 percent of the total lot area unless a more specific provision applies.[Source 2] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.376 In all commercial zones (which can include mixed-use with multifamily), no more than 145 spaces per lot may be provided as surface or flexible-use parking, and in multifamily zones commercial uses are limited to no more than ten parking spaces per business establishment.[Source 9] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.790 In pedestrian-designated zones, surface parking is specifically prohibited abutting the street lot line along a principal pedestrian street.[Source 4] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.345 In some downtown mixed residential/commercial (DMR) areas, accessory surface parking is outright prohibited in DMR/R zones and only allowed in DMR/C zones under specified conditions and space limits.[Source 5] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.592 Director’s Rule 9-2002 further excludes any multifamily parking, access, or accessory structures from being located on the single-family–zoned portion of a lot that is partly single-family and partly multifamily.[Source 12] DIR — seattle_dir_rules, § 9-2002, p.2

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 1/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> In Seattle, surface parking in multifamily (Lowrise, Midrise, Highrise) zones is tightly controlled—both in terms of where it is allowed and where it is explicitly prohibited. --- # Where surface parking *is permitted* Surface parking may generally be located: ### 1) In side yards * Allowed beside the building, subject to screening and landscaping requirements ### 2) In rear yards * The primary preferred location * Especially when accessed from an alley ### 3) Within or under structures * Not "surface parking" strictly speaking, but: * Parking under buildings (e.g., tuck-under) is encouraged over open surface lots --- # Where surface parking is *NOT permitted (excluded areas)* ### 1) Front yard (major restriction) * Prohibited in the required front yard * This is one of the strictest rules 👉 Intent: maintain a pedestrian-friendly streetscape and avoid "parking-dominated" frontages --- ### 2) Between the street and the principal structure * Even outside the strict front yard definition: * Parking is generally not allowed between the building and the street 👉 The building must "front" the street, not parking --- ### 3) Required landscaping areas * Parking cannot occupy required landscaping buffers or screening areas --- ### 4) Certain setbacks and screening zones * Parking is restricted or prohibited: * Near residentially zoned adjacent lots * Without required screening, setbacks, or separation --- ### 5) Environmentally critical areas (if present) * Not allowed in: * Steep slopes * Wetlands * Required buffers --- # Additional constraints (important in practice) Even where parking *is allowed*, it must: * Be screened from streets and adjacent residential uses * Meet setback requirements * Often be accessed from alleys where available * Comply with maximum width limits for curb cuts --- # Big-picture rule (how to think about it) Seattle's multifamily zoning follows a clear hierarchy: > Rear/side = acceptable > Front/street-facing = strongly discouraged or prohibited --- # Practical takeaway If you're designing a multifamily project: * Plan parking behind or beside the building * Avoid relying on front yard or street-facing surface parking—it will almost always be rejected * Expect to use: * Alley access * Structured or partially enclosed parking to make the site work --- If you want, I can sketch a compliant vs. non-compliant site layout—this is one of the most common design pitfalls in Seattle multifamily projects.

### Q012. What specific types of development projects are entirely exempt from the requirements to provide pedestrian access and circulation improvements (such as sidewalks and curbs)?

**Reference answer:** Pedestrian access and circulation improvements are not required for the following: Change of use; Alterations to existing structures; Additions to existing structures that are exempt from environmental review; Construction of a detached structure accessory to an existing dwelling unit (with a recorded no-protest agreement); Construction of one dwelling unit on a lot (with a no-protest agreement and meeting specific site conditions); Expansions of surface parking, outdoor storage, outdoor sales and outdoor display of rental equipment of less than 20 percent of the parking, storage, sales or display area, or number of parking spaces; certain minor additions in the MML zone; and Construction of a new nonresidential structure of up to 4,000 square feet of gross floor area if the structure is at least 50 feet from any lot line abutting an existing street that does not have pedestrian improvements.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 3/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> The following project types are entirely exempt from pedestrian access and circulation improvements (no sidewalks, curbs, curb ramps, or walkways required): - Accessory dwelling units (ADUs) – no public street improvements may be required as a permit condition, other than those required by state or federal law; ADUs are also excluded from dwelling unit counts used to calculate required street improvements. `SMC 23.53.003` - Change of use projects. `SMC 23.53.006.F.1.a` - Alterations to existing structures (no new structure, just alteration). `SMC 23.53.006.F.1.b` - Additions to existing structures that are exempt from environmental review. `SMC 23.53.006.F.1.c` - Expansions of surface parking, outdoor storage, outdoor sales, or outdoor display of rental equipment where the expansion is less than 20 percent of the existing area or number of spaces. `SMC 23.53.006.F.1.f` All other listed exceptions in `SMC 23.53.006.F.1` either require a no‑protest agreement or apply only in specific zones/conditions and therefore are not “entirely exempt” in the same unconditional way.

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 2/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> The SMC lists several project types that are fully exempt from providing pedestrian access and circulation improvements (e.g., sidewalks, curbs, curb ramps): 1. Change of use projects (no new pedestrian improvements required). `[Source 1] SMC — smc_chapters, § 23.53.006, p.761` 2. Alterations to existing structures. `[Source 1] SMC — smc_chapters, § 23.53.006, p.761` 3. Additions to existing structures that are exempt from environmental review. `[Source 1] SMC — smc_chapters, § 23.53.006, p.761` 4. Construction of a detached accessory structure to an existing dwelling unit in any zone, if the owner records a no‑protest agreement to future pedestrian access and circulation improvements. `[Source 6] SMC — smc_chapters, § 23.53.006, p.761` All other “reduced improvement” project types cited (e.g., small nonresidential structures, certain artist’s studio dwellings, one or two dwelling units) remain subject to pedestrian access and circulation requirements in §23.53.006 even if exempt from some dedication or street improvement standards. `[Source 2] SMC — smc_chapters, § 23.53.020, p.774; [Source 5] SMC — smc_chapters, § 23.53.015, p.767`

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 1/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> In Seattle, requirements to install pedestrian access and circulation improvements (like sidewalks, curbs, and related frontage work) generally come from right-of-way improvement rules administered by SDOT. However, some categories of development are fully exempt from having to provide those improvements. Here are the key project types that are entirely exempt: --- # 1) Interior-only work (no exterior/site impact) * Projects limited strictly to: * Interior remodels * Tenant improvements * No change to site use, footprint, or access 👉 No trigger for frontage or pedestrian improvements --- # 2) Minor alterations and repairs * Small-scale work such as: * Repair or replacement of existing elements * Maintenance activities * As long as there is no expansion or intensification of use --- # 3) Mechanical, electrical, and plumbing (MEP) upgrades * Examples: * HVAC replacement * Electrical upgrades * Plumbing work 👉 These do not affect pedestrian circulation or street interface --- # 4) Change of use with no physical changes * If a building changes use but: * No construction or site modification occurs * Then pedestrian improvements are typically not required --- # 5) Very small additions (below threshold) * Additions under certain size/value thresholds (as defined by SDOT rules) * These are treated as de minimis impacts 👉 Thresholds are periodically updated, so they must be checked per current Director's Rules --- # 6) Temporary uses and short-term permits * Construction staging * Temporary structures 👉 These are not permanent developments and do not trigger permanent sidewalk upgrades --- # 7) Projects with no frontage or no impact to right-of-way * For example: * Work on parcels with no abutting improved street frontage * Or where no change in access, density, or intensity occurs --- # Important nuance (this is where people get tripped up) Most projects are NOT exempt. Even relatively small developments can trigger requirements if they: * Increase floor area * Add dwelling units * Intensify use * Modify access points or driveways --- # How the City evaluates this The Seattle Department of Transportation looks at: * Change in use or intensity * Impact on pedestrian demand * Whether the project is an "alteration" vs. "new development" --- # Practical takeaway True exemptions are limited to projects that: > Do not meaningfully change how the site interacts with the public right-of-way If your project: * Adds units * Expands floor area * Changes access → You should assume pedestrian improvements will be required --- If you want, I can help you test a specific project (e.g., ADU, multifamily infill, commercial TI) against Seattle's thresholds—this is often where hidden costs show up.

### Q013. Under what specific exceptions is vehicular access to parking permitted through a required yard abutting a street?

**Reference answer:** Access to parking is permitted through a required yard abutting a street only if the Director determines that one of the following conditions exists: There is no alley improved to the standards of subsection 23.53.030.C and no unimproved alley in common usage; Existing topography does not permit alley access; At least 50 percent of alley frontage abuts property in a non-residential zone; The alley is used for loading or unloading by an existing non-residential use; Due to the relationship of the alley to the street system, use of the alley for parking access would create a significant safety hazard; Parking access must be from the street in order to provide access to a parking space that complies with the Seattle Building Code; or Providing alley access would require removal of a tree on private property that is a Tier 1 or Tier 2 tree.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> Vehicular access to parking is permitted through a required yard abutting a street only if the Director determines that at least one of these conditions exists in `NR1`, `NR2`, or `NR3` zones: 1. No usable alley - There is no alley improved to `23.53.030.C` standards, and no unimproved alley in common usage that currently provides access to parking on the lot or on adjacent lots in the same block. `[SMC 23.44.016.B.2.a]` 2. Topography prevents alley access - Existing topography does not permit alley access. `[SMC 23.44.016.B.2.b]` 3. Predominantly non-residential alley frontage - At least 50 percent of the alley frontage abuts property in a non-residential zone. `[SMC 23.44.016.B.2.c]` 4. Alley used for non-residential loading - The alley is used for loading or unloading by an existing non-residential use. `[SMC 23.44.016.B.2.d]` 5. Alley access would be a significant safety hazard - Due to the relationship of the alley to the street system, use of the alley for parking access would create a significant safety hazard. `[SMC 23.44.016.B.2.e]` 6. Street access needed to meet Building Code parking standards - Parking access must be from the street in order to provide access to a parking space that complies with the Seattle Building Code, Chapter 11. `[SMC 23.44.016.B.2.f]` 7. Alley access would require removal of a protected tree - Providing alley access would require removal of a Tier 1 or Tier 2 tree on private property, as defined in `25.11.130`. `[SMC 23.44.016.B.2.g]`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 3/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> Vehicular access to parking through a required yard abutting a street is allowed only if the Director determines at least one of these conditions exists: 1. There is no alley improved to the standards of SMC 23.53.030.C and no unimproved alley in common usage that currently provides access to parking on the lot or on adjacent lots in the same block. 2. Existing topography does not permit alley access. 3. At least 50 percent of the alley frontage abuts property in a non-residential zone. 4. The alley is used for loading or unloading by an existing non-residential use. [Source 1] SMC — smc_chapters, § 23.44.016.B, p.169

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 2/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> In Seattle, the general rule is that vehicular access (driveways) to parking is *not allowed* through a required yard that abuts a street—especially in residential and multifamily zones—because it conflicts with pedestrian-oriented frontage design. However, the code provides specific, limited exceptions where this is allowed: --- # Key exceptions allowing vehicular access through a required street-facing yard ### 1) When no feasible alternative access exists * If access from: * An alley, or * Another street frontage is not available or not feasible, the City may allow driveway access through the required yard. 👉 This is the most common exception, but it must be justified. --- ### 2) Lots without alley access * If the lot: * Does not abut an alley, and * Has no other reasonable access point → A driveway through the required yard may be permitted --- ### 3) Corner lots (limited flexibility) * On corner lots: * Access may be allowed from the less pedestrian-oriented street frontage * The City often prefers: * Access from the secondary street, not the primary frontage --- ### 4) Existing (legal nonconforming) access * If a driveway: * Existed legally prior to current regulations → It may continue to be used 👉 However, expanding or relocating it may trigger compliance with current rules --- ### 5) Topographic or site constraints * If site conditions such as: * Steep slopes * Irregular lot shape * Critical areas make other access infeasible → The Director may allow access through the required yard --- ### 6) Very limited-width driveway crossings * Even when allowed, the driveway must: * Be minimized in width * Meet curb cut and access standards from Seattle Department of Transportation 👉 The City tries to reduce pedestrian conflict --- # Big-picture rule Seattle's policy is: > "Access parking without compromising the pedestrian environment—only use the required yard as a last resort." --- # Practical takeaway If you're planning access through a required street-facing yard: * Expect to prove that no better option exists * Expect scrutiny from both: * SDCI (land use) * SDOT (right-of-way access) This is not a default allowance—it's a justified exception. --- If you want, I can evaluate a specific lot (dimensions, alley presence, slope) and tell you whether Seattle would likely approve a driveway through the required yard.

### Q014. Can the required parking for a daytime non-residential use be supplied by the parking provided for a nighttime or Sunday use?

**Reference answer:** Yes, up to 90 percent of the required parking for a daytime use may be supplied by the off-street parking provided by a nighttime or Sunday use and vice-versa, when authorized by the Director, except that this may be increased to 100 percent when the nighttime or Sunday use is a religious facility. This exception is conditional upon the applicant showing that there is no substantial conflict in the principal operating hours of the uses for which the sharing of parking to satisfy required parking is proposed

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> Yes. Up to 90 percent of the required parking for a daytime use may be supplied by off‑street parking provided by a nighttime or Sunday use, and vice versa, when authorized by the Director; this may be increased to 100 percent when the nighttime or Sunday use is a religious facility. The applicant must show there is no substantial conflict in the principal operating hours of the uses for which the shared parking is proposed. `SMC 23.54.020.G.3.a` `SMC 23.54.020.G.3.c` `SMC 23.54.020.G.3.d`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> Yes. Seattle’s Land Use Code allows required parking for a daytime non‑residential use to be met by off‑street parking provided for a nighttime or Sunday use, subject to conditions. “Daytime” and “nighttime or Sunday” uses are defined in SMC 23.54.020 (e.g., most commercial, storage, and manufacturing uses are daytime; religious facilities, entertainment uses, and eating and drinking establishments are nighttime/Sunday) [Source 2] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.822. Up to 90% of the required parking for a daytime use may be supplied by the off‑street parking of a nighttime or Sunday use, and vice versa, and this can be increased to 100% when the nighttime/Sunday use is a religious facility, when authorized by the Director [Source 1] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.822. The applicant must demonstrate that there is no substantial conflict in the principal operating hours of the uses for which the shared parking is proposed [Source 1] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.822.

> Yes—in Seattle, required parking for a daytime non-residential use *can* be supplied by parking used for a nighttime or Sunday use, but only under specific shared-parking provisions. --- # How this works (shared parking concept) Seattle allows shared (or joint) use of parking when: * Different uses have peak demand at different times, and * The same parking spaces can serve both without conflict 👉 A classic example: * Office (daytime peak) * Church or entertainment use (evening/Sunday peak) --- # Key conditions that must be met ### 1) Non-overlapping peak demand * The uses must have distinct peak hours * The City must determine that: * Daytime demand will not conflict with nighttime/Sunday demand --- ### 2) Formal shared parking agreement * A recorded agreement (easement or covenant) is typically required * It must: * Guarantee continued availability of the shared spaces * Bind current and future property owners --- ### 3) Director approval * The Seattle Department of Construction and Inspections Director must approve the arrangement * They evaluate: * Hours of operation * Parking demand studies (if needed) * Potential conflicts --- ### 4) Location and accessibility * The shared parking must be: * Reasonably accessible to both uses * Located within allowable distance limits (if off-site) --- ### 5) Compliance with general parking standards * Even when shared: * The parking must still meet design, access, and safety standards --- # Important limitations * This is not automatic—you must demonstrate compatibility * If hours begin to overlap (e.g., extended business hours), the City may: * Require additional parking * Revoke or modify the approval --- # ### Q015. How does RCW 36.70A.681's prohibition on ADU parking requirements connect to Seattle's SMC 23.53.003 ?

**Reference answer:** RCW 36.70A.681 and Seattle's SMC 23.53.003 are directly connected through their shared regulatory limits on what municipalities can demand from property owners when permitting Accessory Dwelling Units (ADUs). While RCW 36.70A.681 sets strict limitations on the off-street parking that a city or county can require for an ADU (such as prohibiting parking requirements for ADUs within one-half mile of a major transit stop), the very same state statute also contains a mandate that a city or county may not require public street improvements as a condition of permitting accessory dwelling units. Seattle's SMC 23.53.003 serves as the local municipal implementation of this specific state-level prohibition. It establishes the following rules to align with the state code: Exemption from Street Improvements: It dictates that no public street improvements shall be required as a condition of permitting an ADU for construction, conversion, expansion, or change of use, except for those explicitly required by other state or federal laws. Exclusion from Dwelling Counts: To ensure these street improvement exemptions are properly applied to the rest of the property, SMC 23.53.003 mandates that ADUs must be entirely excluded from the dwelling unit counts used to calculate required street improvements for a lot. In short, while RCW 36.70A.681 broadly restricts local jurisdictions from imposing both burdensome parking minimums and street improvement mandates on ADUs, Seattle's SMC 23.53.003 represents the specific local code enacting the street improvement portion of that state law.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> RCW 36.70A.681 and SMC 23.53.003 work together so that Seattle both cannot require off‑street parking for ADUs in many locations and also cannot condition ADU permits on building new public street improvements. ### State ADU parking limits - Cities and counties must allow at least two ADUs per lot in urban growth areas and may not require more restrictive standards for ADUs than for principal units, including parking-related standards. `[RCW 36.70A.681]` - For ADUs, local governments may not require off‑street parking within one‑half mile walking distance of a major transit stop, and even outside that area they may not require more than one space per ADU on lots ≤ 6,000 sq ft or more than two spaces per ADU on lots > 6,000 sq ft, unless they obtain a certified empirical safety study or fall within the airport exception. `[RCW 36.70A.680]` - RCW 36.70A.680–.681 requirements supersede, preempt, and invalidate any conflicting local development regulations where a city has not yet updated its code, and any compliant local regulations must still stay within these state parking caps. `[RCW 36.70A.680]` ### Seattle’s ADU street‑improvement rule - Seattle expressly provides that no public street improvements (other than those required by state or federal law) shall be required as a condition of permitting ADUs for construction, conversion, expansion, change of use, or other development methods. `[SMC 23.53.003]` - For purposes of calculating required street improvements under Chapter `23.53`, ADUs are excluded from dwelling unit counts, so adding ADUs cannot trigger higher local street‑improvement obligations. `[SMC 23.53.003]` ### How they connect - State law bars Seattle from imposing excessive or transit‑area off‑street parking requirements on ADUs; Seattle goes further by also barring local public street‑improvement exactions tied to ADU permitting, except where higher‑level (state/federal) law independently requires them. `[RCW 36.70A.680]` `[RCW 36.70A.681]` `[SMC 23.53.003]` - Because RCW 36.70A.680–.681 preempt conflicting local rules, Seattle’s choice in `SMC 23.53.003` to eliminate ADU‑triggered street‑improvement requirements is consistent with the state’s intent to reduce regulatory barriers to ADUs and cannot be undercut by more onerous local parking or street‑improvement conditions. `[RCW 36.70A.680]` `[RCW 36.70A.681]` `[SMC 23.53.003]`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 1/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> RCW 36.70A.681 itself (the “limitations on local regulation” section) does not contain the ADU parking prohibition language in the excerpts provided; instead, the specific state prohibition on requiring off‑street parking for ADUs near transit is in RCW 36.70A.698, and RCW 36.70A.697/680–681 together make those state ADU standards preempt conflicting local codes. RCW 36.70A.697(2) and 36.70A.680(1)(b) explicitly state that, where a city’s development regulations conflict with the state ADU requirements (including the ADU parking prohibition in RCW 36.70A.698), the state provisions “supersede, preempt, and invalidate” the local regulations. [Source 11] [Source 7]. [Source 2] [Source 11]. In addition, RCW 36.70A.622 and 36.70A.817 further limit how cities can structure residential parking requirements generally (e.g., they cannot require garages to meet minimum parking, must count tandem spaces in a specified way, and cannot require off‑street parking for certain residential project types), which also constrain how SMC 23.53.003 can be applied to ADUs and other housing. [Source 8] [Source 4]. ### Q016. What state law does WAC 365-196-875 reference for residential parking minimums, and what obligation does this create for Seattle?

**Reference answer:** WAC 365-196-875 specifically references RCW 36.70A.620 and RCW 36.70A.622 for its minimum residential parking requirements. Because Seattle is a city that plans under the state's Growth Management Act (RCW 36.70A.040), WAC 365-196-875 and RCW 36.70A.622 obligate the city to enforce specific, standardized land use regulations for residential parking. Under these state laws, Seattle must comply with the following mandates when regulating residential development: No Garage Mandates: Seattle cannot require garages or carports as a way to meet minimum residential parking requirements. Enclosure Flexibility: Parking spaces that count toward minimum requirements may be either enclosed or unenclosed. Tandem Parking Allowances: Tandem parking spaces must be counted toward minimum parking requirements at a rate of one space for every 20 linear feet (provided turning radius provisions are met). Gravel Surface Grandfathering: The existence of legally nonconforming gravel surfacing in designated parking areas cannot be used as a reason to prohibit the use of those spaces to meet local parking standards, up to a maximum of six spaces. Maximum Space Dimensions: Seattle cannot require parking spaces to exceed 8 feet in width by 20 feet in length, except for required parking for people with disabilities. Tree Retention Protections: Seattle cannot require off-street parking as a condition for permitting a residential project if doing so would make the development infeasible due to the need to retain significant trees. Grass Block Pavers: Parking spaces constructed with grass block pavers must be permitted to count toward minimum parking requirements. Additionally, the documents note that existing parking spaces that did not conform to WAC and RCW size requirements as of June 6, 2024, are not required to be modified or resized (except for ADA compliance), and that these specific state parking mandates do not apply to portions of cities located within a one-mile radius of a commercial airport with at least 9,000,000 annual enplanements.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> Seattle must apply the state residential parking minimum rules in `RCW 36.70A.620` and `RCW 36.70A.622` because `WAC 365-196-875` makes them mandatory for GMA cities. ### Referenced state law - `WAC 365-196-875(1)` states that for counties and cities planning under `RCW 36.70A.040`, “the minimum residential parking requirements of RCW 36.70A.620 and 36.70A.622 shall apply.” `[WAC 365-196-875]` - `RCW 36.70A.622` (decodified version of former `RCW 36.70A.620`) sets detailed statewide rules for residential parking, including: - Garages and carports may not be required to meet minimum residential parking requirements. `[RCW 36.70A.620]` - Enclosed or unenclosed spaces must count toward minimum parking. `[RCW 36.70A.620]` - Tandem spaces must count at 1 space per 20 linear feet with turning radius provisions. `[RCW 36.70A.620]` - Existing legal gravel areas up to six spaces must be allowed to count toward standards. `[RCW 36.70A.620]` - Maximum stall size: jurisdictions may not require stalls larger than 8 feet by 20 feet, except ADA spaces. `[RCW 36.70A.620]` - Grass block paver spaces must be allowed to count. `[RCW 36.70A.620]` - Tree-retention override: in GMA counties and cities > 6,000 population, off‑street parking may not be required if tree‑retention compliance would otherwise make a residential project infeasible. `[RCW 36.70A.620]` - Airport-area exception within 1 mile of a large commercial airport. `[RCW 36.70A.620]` ### Resulting obligation for Seattle - Seattle is a city planning under `RCW 36.70A.040`, so it is within the class of jurisdictions to which `WAC 365-196-875(1)` applies. `[WAC 365-196-875]` - Because `WAC 365-196-875(1)` says the minimum residential parking requirements of `RCW 36.70A.620` and `RCW 36.70A.622` “shall apply,” Seattle must ensure its land use and parking regulations conform to those state minimums (e.g., cannot require garages to meet minimums, must count tandem and grass‑paver spaces, cannot require stalls larger than 8'×20', and must apply the tree‑retention infeasibility rule, subject to the airport‑area carve‑out). `[WAC 365-196-875]` `[RCW 36.70A.620]`

> WAC 365-196-875 expressly states that minimum residential parking requirements are those in RCW 36.70A.620 and RCW 36.70A.622 for counties and cities planning under RCW 36.70A.040.[Source 1] These provisions apply to “counties and cities planning under RCW 36.70A.040,” which includes Seattle as a fully planning GMA city, so Seattle must ensure its development and zoning regulations incorporate and comply with the residential parking minimum (and related transit‑area reductions) established in RCW 36.70A.620 and 36.70A.622.[Source 1]
