### Q017. How does Washington State's parking standard for middle housing compare to Seattle's obligations for duplexes and fourplexes?

**Reference answer:** Under Washington State law, "middle housing", which explicitly includes duplexes and fourplexes is subject to strict caps on how much parking a municipality can demand. The state's standards mandate that cities cannot require: Any off-street parking for middle housing located within one-half mile walking distance of a major transit stop. More than one off-street parking space per unit on lots that are 6,000 square feet or smaller. More than two off-street parking spaces per unit on lots larger than 6,000 square feet. Seattle's municipal obligations directly reflect and comply with these state-level restrictions. Seattle's land use code mirrors the state's transit-based exemption by mandating that there is no minimum parking requirement for middle housing located within one-half mile walking distance of a major transit stop. For areas where transit-proximity exemptions do not apply, Seattle generally requires one parking space per dwelling unit for multifamily residential uses. This standard easily satisfies the state's mandates, as it perfectly aligns with the state's cap of one space per unit for smaller lots and is actually less restrictive than the state's two-space cap for larger lots. Furthermore, Seattle actually applies zero-parking minimums more broadly than the state's baseline middle housing protections require, mandating no minimum parking for all residential uses within urban centers, the Station Area Overlay District, and qualifying urban villages located in frequent transit service areas.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> State law caps how much off‑street parking Seattle can require for middle housing; Seattle’s duplex/fourplex rules must fit within those state maximums. ### State middle‑housing parking caps - Cities subject to `RCW 36.70A.635` (including Seattle) shall not require off‑street parking for middle housing within one‑half mile walking distance of a major transit stop. `[RCW 36.70A.630]` - Outside that half‑mile area, cities shall not require more than: - 1 off‑street space per unit on lots no greater than 6,000 sq ft (pre‑subdivision). `[RCW 36.70A.630]` - 2 off‑street spaces per unit on lots greater than 6,000 sq ft (pre‑subdivision). `[RCW 36.70A.630]` - These limits can be overridden only if Commerce certifies an empirical safety study under `RCW 36.70A.635(7)(a)`. `[RCW 36.70A.630]` - Commerce’s parking rule confirms that for middle housing, cities may not require any off‑street parking within ½‑mile of a major transit stop, and may not exceed the 1‑space and 2‑space caps above; it also directs that where these caps conflict with older minimum‑parking statutes, the least restrictive requirement applies. `[WAC 365-196-875] [WAC 365-196-890]` ### Seattle duplex/fourplex obligations - Seattle must allow at least 2–6 units per lot on lots zoned predominantly for residential use, depending on city size, proximity to major transit stops, and affordability; duplexes and fourplexes are among the “middle housing” types that must be allowed to meet these minimum densities. `[RCW 36.70A.630]` - For all such middle housing (including duplexes and fourplexes), Seattle may not impose standards more restrictive than for detached single‑family residences, but may apply the same objective standards (setbacks, lot coverage, stormwater, clearing, tree canopy/retention). `[RCW 36.70A.630] [WAC 365-196-890]` - Seattle must apply to middle housing the same development permit and environmental review processes it applies to detached single‑family residences, except where other state laws (shoreline, building, energy, electrical codes) require otherwise. `[RCW 36.70A.630] [WAC 365-196-890]` - For parking specifically, Seattle shall not require: - Any off‑street parking for middle housing (including duplexes/fourplexes) within ½‑mile of a major transit stop. `[RCW 36.70A.630]` - More than 1 space per unit on lots ≤ 6,000 sq ft. `[RCW 36.70A.630]` - More than 2 spaces per unit on lots > 6,000 sq ft. `[RCW 36.70A.630]` - Seattle’s general residential parking framework (e.g., `SMC 23.44.016` requiring access and location standards when parking is required) must be applied to duplexes/fourplexes only within these state caps; Seattle cannot set higher minimums for middle housing than state law allows. `[SMC 23.44.016] [RCW 36.70A.630]` ### Comparison: state vs. Seattle obligations | Agency / Code | Rule Type | Parking Requirement for Middle Housing (incl. duplexes/fourplexes) | Source | |---------------|-----------|---------------------------------------------------------------------|--------| | State – `RCW 36.70A.630` / `RCW 36.70A.635` | Statute (preemptive) | No minimum off‑street parking allowed within ½‑mile of a major transit stop; Cities: * cannot require more parking than these caps, and * in some locations (near transit) cannot require any parking at all. Because "middle housing" is defined to include duplexes, triplexes, and fourplexes ([Justia][2]), these limits directly apply to those housing types. --- # 2) What Seattle is obligated to do for duplexes and fourplexes Seattle, as a GMA city, must ensure its code does not exceed those limits. That creates two key obligations: ### A. For duplexes and fourplexes (as "middle housing") Seattle must: * Allow zero parking minimums near transit * Cap requirements at 1–2 spaces per unit elsewhere, depending on lot size It cannot impose stricter minimums unless it qualifies for a narrow exception (e.g., an evidence-based safety study). --- ### B. Compared to earlier / general parking authority Separate GMA provisions (like RCW 36.70A.620) already limit parking for multifamily housing near transit (e.g., ≤0.75 spaces/unit in some cases) ([Justia][3]), but: * Those rules are conditional and category-specific (affordable housing, transit proximity, etc.) * The middle housing statute is broader and more categorical: * It applies statewide to duplexes/fourplexes as a class * It imposes hard caps and outright prohibitions, not just conditional limits --- # 3) Bottom-line comparison | موضوع | State middle housing standard | Seattle's obligation | | ------------ | ------------------------------------- | --------------------------------- | | Scope | Applies to duplexes, fourplexes, etc. | Must apply to same housing types | | Near transit | No parking minimums allowed | Must allow zero minimums | | Small lots | Max 1 space/unit | Cannot exceed 1 | | Larger lots | Max 2 spaces/unit | Cannot exceed 2 | | Legal force | Binding state ceiling | Mandatory compliance (preemption) | --- ## Key takeaway Washington's middle housing law effectively sets a ceiling and, in some cases, a floor of zero for parking requirements on duplexes and fourplexes. For Seattle, that means: * It must allow these housing types with little or no required parking, and * It cannot maintain or adopt stricter minimums, even if local policy preferences would favor them. If you want, I can walk through how Seattle's current zoning code compares line-by-line to these caps—that's where the real compliance questions usually show up. ### Q018. How do Washington State's ADU parking requirements differ from Seattle's implementation in SMC?

**Reference answer:** Washington State law establishes strict limits on the maximum amount of off-street parking a local municipality can require for accessory dwelling units (ADUs), while Seattle's Municipal Code (SMC) goes a step further by removing ADU parking mandates entirely. Under Washington State law (RCW 36.70A.681 and WAC 365-196-875), municipalities are prohibited from requiring: Any off-street parking for ADUs located within a one-half mile walking distance of a major transit stop. More than one off-street parking space per ADU on lots smaller than 6,000 square feet. More than two off-street parking spaces per ADU on lots greater than 6,000 square feet. Seattle's implementation differs because it is significantly less restrictive than the state's baseline regulations. Instead of applying parking caps based on lot size or transit proximity, Seattle's land use code completely exempts ADUs from all parking minimums. Both SMC 23.42.022 (governing accessory dwelling units) and the general residential parking tables in SMC 23.54.015 explicitly state that no off-street motor vehicle parking is required for an accessory dwelling unit anywhere in the city

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 2/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> State law broadly limits when cities can require off-street parking for ADUs, and Seattle’s SMC must comply but may be more restrictive where allowed. ### State ADU parking rules - Cities planning under the GMA may not require off‑street parking for ADUs within one‑quarter mile of a major transit stop, unless they make an evidence‑based finding that on‑street parking is infeasible due to lack of capacity, physical impediments, or similar reasons. `[RCW 36.70A.698]` - A city that adopted or substantively amended ADU regulations in the four years before June 11, 2020 is temporarily exempt from the above transit‑based parking prohibition. `[RCW 36.70A.698]` - Separately, for jurisdictions subject to the newer ADU statute, a city or county may not require off‑street parking for ADUs within one‑half mile walking distance of a major transit stop, and may not require more than one space per ADU on lots ≤ 6,000 sq ft or more than two spaces per ADU on lots > 6,000 sq ft, with limited safety/airport exceptions. `[RCW 36.70A.680]` - Commerce’s implementing rule requires that local ADU codes “align parking with the requirements of” the minimum residential parking WAC, which incorporates the same ADU parking caps and transit‑area prohibitions. `[WAC 365-196-880]` `[WAC 365-196-875]` - Under `RCW 36.70A.697(2)`, the state ADU requirements supersede, preempt, and invalidate any conflicting local development regulations. `[RCW 36.70A.697]` ### Seattle SMC treatment of parking (no ADU‑specific override shown) - Seattle’s code sections provided here regulate parking quantity, location, and design generally, but do not contain ADU‑specific parking ratios or explicit ADU exemptions from minimum parking. `[SMC 23.44.016]` `[SMC 23.54.030]` - **ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 3/5, Faithfulness 2/5

> The difference is pretty stark: Washington State largely eliminates local discretion on ADU parking, while Seattle's SMC reflects that preemption by effectively removing parking requirements altogether (and had already moved in that direction even before the latest state laws). --- # 1) Washington State ADU parking standard Under recent GMA amendments (primarily RCW 36.70A.680–.681 from HB 1337 and later updates), the state sets strong limits on when parking can be required for ADUs: * No parking minimums within 1⁄2 mile of major transit ([ADUPlanet][1]) * More broadly, state guidance requires cities to "modify parking requirements based on distance from a major transit stop and/or lot size" ([Washington State Department of Commerce][2]) * In practice (and widely summarized in guidance and secondary sources), jurisdictions generally cannot require off-street parking for ADUs, especially in urban areas ([seattlehomeway.com][3]) ### Key takeaway (state level) * Parking for ADUs is highly restricted or prohibited, especially near transit * Local governments have very limited ability to impose minimums at all --- # 2) Seattle's implementation in the Seattle Municipal Code (SMC) Seattle's current approach is even simpler: * No off-street parking is required for ADUs under Seattle's land use rules ([ADUPlanet][4]) * The city explicitly notes that ADUs are exempt from parking requirements in many cases ([Seattle][5]) This reflects Seattle's earlier reforms (2019 ordinance) that: * Eliminated parking minimums for both attached (AADUs) and detached (DADUs) citywide * Did not condition that removal on transit proximity --- # 3) Direct comparison | Issue | Washington State requirement | Seattle SMC implementation | | ----------------- | ------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------- | | Baseline rule | Parking requirements must be limited | No parking required at all | | Near transit | Parking minimums prohibited | Already zero everywhere | | Away from transit | May allow limited requirements (subject to constraints) | Still zero | | Local discretion | Narrow, constrained by statute | Seattle chose maximum deregulation | --- # 4) What this means legally * State law sets a ceiling: cities generally *cannot require parking* for ADUs in many cases (especially near transit). * Seattle goes further: it does not require parking anywhere, which is fully compliant because: * Cities are free to be less restrictive than the state ceiling, but * Cannot be more restrictive. --- # Bottom line * Washington State: "You usually can't require parking for ADUs—especially near transit." * Seattle (SMC): "We don't require parking for ADUs at all, anywhere." So unlike middle housing (where cities must carefully stay under state caps), Seattle's ADU rules are already at or beyond the state's most permissive standard, leaving essentially no compliance gap on parking. ### Q019. What overlapping state and local regulations govern off-street and bicycle parking for a new fourplex in Seattle?

**Reference answer:** A fourplex is classified as "middle housing" under both Washington State law and the Seattle Municipal Code (SMC). Because of this classification, the development of a new fourplex in Seattle is governed by specific, overlapping state and local regulations designed to limit parking requirements and provide design flexibility. Off-Street Parking Requirements and Caps Washington State law places strict caps on how much off-street parking a city can require for middle housing, and Seattle's local code aligns with these mandates: Transit Proximity: Under RCW 36.70A.635 and WAC 365-196-875, Seattle cannot require any off-street parking if the fourplex is located within a one-half mile walking distance of a major transit stop. Seattle explicitly implements this state mandate in Table B for 23.54.015 (Row Q), requiring no minimum parking for middle housing near major transit stops. Broad Local Exemptions: Seattle goes further than state law by removing minimum parking requirements for residential uses located anywhere within urban centers, the Station Area Overlay District, and qualifying urban villages located in frequent transit service areas. State Maximums vs. Local Minimums: In areas where transit exemptions do not apply, state law dictates that cities cannot require more than one off-street space per unit on lots 6,000 square feet or smaller, or more than two off-street spaces per unit on lots larger than 6,000 square feet. Seattle complies with these caps by generally requiring only one space per dwelling unit for multifamily housing where no other exemptions exist. Off-Street Parking Design and Enforcement Rules When off-street parking is provided for residential development like a fourplex, state law (RCW 36.70A.622 and WAC 365-196-875) preempts local jurisdictions from imposing overly burdensome design rules. Seattle must adhere to the following state standards: No Garage Mandates: Seattle cannot require the construction of garages or carports to meet minimum parking requirements. Enclosure Flexibility: Provided parking spaces can be either enclosed or unenclosed. Tandem Parking: Seattle must count tandem parking spaces toward minimum requirements at a rate of one space for every 20 linear feet, provided turning radius provisions are met. Space Dimensions: Seattle cannot require parking spaces to exceed 8 feet in width by 20 feet in length, except for required ADA accessible parking. Surface Materials: Spaces constructed with grass block pavers must count toward parking requirements. Tree Protections: Seattle cannot require off-street parking as a permit condition if compliance would make the project infeasible due to the need to retain significant trees. Bicycle Parking Regulations While Seattle generally requires one long-term bicycle parking space per dwelling unit for standard multifamily structures, middle housing is explicitly exempt from this requirement. According to Footnote 8 of Seattle's bicycle parking tables (Table D for 23.54.015), no bicycle parking is required for middle housing, which includes fourplexes. If the developer chooses to provide bicycle parking voluntarily, however, the spaces must meet standard design requirements such as being in highly visible, safe, and convenient locations, and accommodating the locking of the frame and both wheels.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> State law sets maximums and prohibitions on requiring off‑street parking for this fourplex; Seattle can be more permissive (allow less parking) but not more restrictive where state caps apply. ### Applicable project type - A fourplex in Seattle is “middle housing” because it is 2–6 units on a lot zoned predominantly for residential use. `[RCW 36.70A.635(1)]` - Seattle is a city planning under `RCW 36.70A.040`, so the middle‑housing parking limits and related WAC rules apply. `[RCW 36.70A.635(1)]` `[WAC 365-196-875(1)]` --- ### State off‑street motor‑vehicle parking limits (fourplex as middle housing) - Cities planning under `RCW 36.70A.040` shall not require off‑street parking for middle housing within one‑half mile walking distance of a major transit stop. `[RCW 36.70A.635(6)(d)]` - Outside that ½‑mile area, cities shall not require more than: - 1 off‑street space per unit on lots no greater than 6,000 sq ft (pre‑subdivision). `[RCW 36.70A.635(6)(e)]` - 2 off‑street spaces per unit on lots greater than 6,000 sq ft (pre‑subdivision). `[RCW 36.70A.635(6)(f)]` - These limits do not apply: - Where Commerce has certified a local empirical safety study justifying higher minimums. `[RCW 36.70A.635(7)(a)]` - Within 1 mile of a commercial airport with ≥ 9,000,000 annual enplanements. `[RCW 36.70A.635(7)(b)]` - Commerce’s implementing rule repeats these caps and clarifies that when state parking limits conflict, the least restrictive applies and the state caps should be treated as maximums that may be reduced. `[WAC 365-196-875(3)]` `[WAC 365-196-875(5)]` `[WAC 365-196-875(6)]` --- ### Other state residential parking constraints that may overlap - For accessory dwelling units (ADUs), cities may not require any off‑street parking within ½ mile of a major transit stop, and may not exceed 1 space per unit on lots ≤ 6,000 sq ft or 2 spaces per unit on larger lots, with the same airport and empirical‑study exceptions. `[RCW 36.70A.680(2)(a)]` `[RCW 36.70A.680(2)(b)]` - For co‑living housing, cities may not require off‑street parking within ½ mile of a major transit stop, and elsewhere may not require more than 0.25 spaces per sleeping unit, subject to the same airport and empirical‑study carve‑outs. `[RCW 36.70A.535(2)(i)]` `[RCW 36.70A.535(3)(a)(ii)]` `[RCW 36.70A.535(3)(b)]` - For certain residential project types (affordable housing, passive house, modular, mass timber), cities may not require any off‑street parking at all, unless they obtain Commerce certification of an empirical safety study; this is a separate, project‑type‑based preemption that can apply even outside transit areas. `[RCW 36.70A.817(1)]` `[RCW 36.70A.817(2)]` --- ### State rules on how minimums must be administered - Cities may not require garages or carports as the way to meet minimum residential parking; spaces that count may be enclosed or unenclosed. `[RCW 36.70A.622(1)(a)]` `[RCW 36.70A.622(1)(b)]` - Tandem spaces must count toward minimums at 1 space per 20 linear feet (with turning radius as needed). `[RCW 36.70A.622(1)(c)]` - Cities may not require stall dimensions to exceed 8 ft by 20 ft (except ADA spaces), and may not disallow use of existing gravel areas (up to 6 spaces) solely because they are gravel. `[RCW 36.70A.622(1)(d)]` `[RCW 36.70A.622(1)(e)]` - Counties and qualifying cities may not require off‑street parking if compliance with tree retention would otherwise make a residential project infeasible. `[RCW 36.70A.622(1)(f)]` - Grass block paver spaces must be allowed to count toward minimums. `[RCW 36.70A.622(1)(g)]` - Commerce’s rule restates these as mandatory local enforcement standards and clarifies that existing nonconforming stalls generally need not be resized. `[WAC 365-196-875(7)]` `[WAC 365-196-875(8)]` --- ### Seattle motor‑vehicle parking rules that overlap - In Downtown zones, Seattle requires no minimum off‑street parking for uses on lots in Downtown zones (with limited International District exceptions), but does impose maximums for non‑residential uses (e.g., 1 space per 1,000 sq ft, or 2 per 1,000 sq ft east of I‑5 for certain uses). `[SMC 23.49.019.A.1]` `[SMC 23.49.019.C.1]` `[SMC 23.49.019.C.2]` - A property owner may seek a Type I reduction or elimination of previously required parking, but bicycle parking cannot be reduced under this provision. `[SMC 23.49.019.A.2]` - In SM‑U zones, Seattle limits above‑grade parking and requires most parking to be below grade or screened and separated by other uses, with limited exceptions for small or shallow lots. `[SMC 23.48.685.A.1]` `[SMC 23.48.685.A.2]` - For rented or leased multifamily units, accessory off‑street parking must be leased separately from the dwelling unit (with exceptions for moderate‑income units and units with individual garages). `[SMC 23.42.070.A.1]` `[SMC 23.42.070.A.2]` `[SMC 23.42.070.A.3]` --- ### Bicycle parking – state vs. Seattle #### State‑level bicycle‑related parking rules - State minimum‑parking statutes and Commerce rules focus on motor‑vehicle stall counts and configuration; none of the provided RCWs or WACs set quantitative bicycle parking minimums for residential projects. `Not found in provided sources` - State bicycle WACs address where bicycles may be parked in public rights‑of‑way or on campuses, not private development standards: - On highways, bicycles must be parked off the roadway in designated areas, on racks, against buildings, or otherwise with least obstruction to pedestrians. `[WAC 308-330-550]` - On certain university campuses, bicycles and scooters must be parked only in designated parking areas and not in buildings, exits, planted areas, or chained to trees, railings, etc. `[WAC 478-116-199(1)]` `[WAC 478-118-290(5)]` #### Seattle bicycle parking requirements for a fourplex - Seattle’s Land Use Code requires bicycle parking by use; Table D of `SMC 23.54.015` sets long‑term and short‑term bicycle parking ratios for many commercial and institutional uses, and the section establishes performance and location standards for residential bicycle parking. `[SMC 23.54.015.K.2]` `[SMC 23.54.015 Table D]` - Long‑term bicycle parking for residential uses must be: - In secure locations (e.g., locked rooms/cages, lockers), with safe access and adequate lighting. `[SMC 23.54.015.K.2.a]` `[SMC visible from the street. `[SMC 23.54.015.K.3.b]` `[SMC 23.54.015.K.2.d]` - Bicycle parking inside dwelling units or on balconies generally does not count toward required bicycle parking, except in a **ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 2/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> For a new fourplex in Seattle, parking is governed by a layered system where state law sets ceilings (and prohibitions) and the Seattle Municipal Code (SMC) fills in design, siting, and bicycle requirements. Here's how those overlap in practice. --- # 1) State law (GMA) — controls *whether and how much* off-street parking you can require The key state provisions are: * RCW 36.70A.620 (general residential parking limits) * RCW 36.70A.622 (middle housing, including fourplexes) * Implemented via WAC 365-196-875 ### What they do for a fourplex Because a fourplex is "middle housing," the state: * Prohibits parking minimums near transit * Caps requirements elsewhere at roughly: * ≤ 1 space/unit (small lots) * ≤ 2 spaces/unit (larger lots) And critically: * These are binding ceilings—Seattle cannot require more parking than this, and in some cases must allow zero parking. 👉 So at the state level, the question is mostly: > *"What's the maximum Seattle is allowed to require?"* --- # 2) Seattle Municipal Code (SMC) — controls *how parking works on the site* Within those state limits, Seattle regulates form, location, and technical standards. ## A. Off-street vehicle parking (SMC Title 23) Core provisions include: * SMC 23.54 (Parking and Loading) — governs: * Number of spaces (subject to state caps) * Dimensions, access, maneuvering, and layout ([Seattle][1]) * Zone-specific rules (e.g., SMC 23.44 for neighborhood residential zones) control: * Where parking can be placed (front yard limits, setbacks, etc.) ([Seattle][2]) ### What this means for a fourplex Even if no parking is required, if you *choose to provide it*: * It must meet: * Driveway and curb cut standards * Stall size and accessibility rules (Seattle Building Code cross-reference) ([Seattle][1]) * Placement is constrained (e.g., limits in front/side yards in residential zones) ([Seattle][2]) 👉 So SMC does not necessarily force parking, but it strictly regulates it if provided. --- ## B. Bicycle parking (SMC 23.54.015) This is where Seattle goes beyond state law. * Bicycle parking is required for new multifamily development, including fourplexes * Must include: * Short-term parking (visitor racks near entrances) * Long-term parking (secure storage for residents) ([Seattle][3]) * Must be: * "Safe, accessible, and convenient" * Installed per city design standards ([Seattle][4]) Seattle also specifies: * Ratios based on number of units or parking supply (historically codified in SMC 23.54.015 tables) * Design details (rack type, spacing, placement near entrances) via SDOT guidance ([streetsillustrated.seattle.gov][5]) 👉 Unlike car parking, bike parking is generally mandatory, not optional. --- # 3) How the layers interact (what actually governs your fourplex) ## Off-street car parking * State law (RCW/WAC) → Sets maximums and bans minimums in some areas * Seattle SMC → Regulates design, access, and placement Result: * You may be allowed to build a fourplex with zero car parking * But if you add parking, it must comply with detailed SMC standards --- ## Bicycle parking * State law → Largely silent (no comparable mandates) * Seattle SMC → Affirmatively requires bike parking Result: * Even if you provide no car parking, you will still need: * Short-term bike racks * Long-term bike storage --- # 4) Bottom line For a Seattle fourplex: * Car parking * Governed by state ceilings (RCW 36.70A.620/.622) * Implemented through SMC siting and design rules * Often not required at all, especially near transit * Bicycle parking * Required by Seattle, independent of state law * Must meet SMC quantity + SDOT design standards --- ## Key insight Washington State law is primarily about removing barriers (limiting parking mandates), while Seattle's code is about managing the physical reality of parking—and it shifts the burden toward bicycle infrastructure instead of car storage. --- If you want, I can map this onto a specific lot (e.g., distance to transit, zoning, lot size) and tell you exactly how many parking and bike spaces your fourplex would need. ### Q020. What is the full set of parking-related requirements a developer must satisfy when permitting an ADU in Seattle?

**Reference answer:** Under the Seattle Municipal Code, no off-street motor vehicle parking is required for an accessory dwelling unit (ADU). While developers are exempt from providing motor vehicle parking, there are a few related regulations and incentives regarding parking and street access when permitting an ADU:Bicycle Parking Exemption: If a developer chooses to provide bicycle parking, up to 35 square feet of gross floor area dedicated to long-term bicycle parking is excluded from the calculation of the ADU's gross floor area limit. Street Improvement Exemptions: No public street improvements can be required as a condition of permitting an ADU for construction, conversion, expansion, or change of use, unless explicitly required by other state or federal laws . Furthermore, ADUs must be entirely excluded from the dwelling unit counts used to calculate required street improvements for a lot. Unit Lot Subdivision Restrictions: If a developer is utilizing a unit lot subdivision, the subdivision cannot result in an ADU being located on a different unit lot than the unit lot of its associated principal dwelling unit.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> - Quantity of motor-vehicle parking - No off-street motor vehicle parking is required for an accessory dwelling unit (ADU). `SMC 23.42.022.I` - Title 23 parking quantity tables (`23.54.015`) still apply to any other uses on the lot, but ADUs themselves have no minimum; where there is a conflict with other Title 23 provisions (other than shoreline), the ADU section controls. `SMC 23.42.022.K` - Location and setbacks for ADU-related parking/garages - ADUs and their appurtenant elements (porches, decks, steps, etc.) follow the yard and setback provisions for principal dwelling units in the underlying zone, except as specifically modified for detached ADUs and alleys. `SMC 23.42.022.E` - In all zones, detached ADUs have no required setback from any lot line that abuts an alley. `SMC 23.42.022.E.1` - In Neighborhood Residential zones, a detached ADU and its appurtenant elements may be in the rear yard if at least 5 feet from any lot line that does not abut an alley; certain projections (chimneys, eaves, bay windows, garden windows) may be closer under the detailed dimensional limits in `23.42.022.E.2.a`. `SMC 23.42.022.E.2` - Detached ADUs in Lowrise zones are excluded from setback averaging and instead follow the minimum setback for a principal dwelling unit. `SMC 23.42.022.E.3` - General single-family/Neighborhood Residential rules still apply to any parking and garages on the lot (for the principal unit or optional spaces serving the ADU), including: - Parking must be on the same lot as the principal use, with limited exceptions. `SMC 23.44.016.C.1` - No more than three vehicles may be parked outdoors on a lot developed with one single-family dwelling. `SMC 23.44.016.C.3` - Parking and garages are generally prohibited within 20 feet of a front lot line and in certain side/rear yards abutting streets, subject to the detailed exceptions in `23.44.016.D` (uphill/downhill yards, terraced garages, etc.). `SMC 23.44.016.D.1` `SMC 23.44.016.D.2` - Access to any required/optional parking - Where off-street parking is required on a Neighborhood Residential lot (for uses other than the ADU), vehicular access must be from an improved street, alley, or easement. `SMC 23.44.016.B.1` - Access through a required yard abutting a street is only allowed if one of the specific conditions is met (e.g., no usable alley, topography prevents alley access, alley safety issues, need to meet Building Code accessibility, or preservation of Tier 1/Tier 2 trees). `SMC 23.44.016.B.2` - General driveway and access standards apply to any new parking serving the principal unit or ADU: - Minimum driveway widths, backing limits, and when backing onto streets is allowed are set in `23.54.030.D.1` (e.g., 10‑foot minimum width for short driveways serving ≤30 spaces; backing onto a street allowed only for ≤5 spaces and not on arterials, with a narrow exception). `SMC 23.54.030.D.1` `SMC 23.54.030.C.2` `SMC 23.54.030.D.1.f` - The number of curb cuts is limited by street frontage length per Tables A and B (e.g., 1 curb cut for ≤80 feet of non‑arterial frontage). `SMC 23.54.030.G, Table A for 23.54.030` `SMC 23.54.030.G, Table B for 23.54.030` - Sight triangle requirements at driveways/garage exits apply, with limited exceptions for small residential lots and alley access. `SMC 23.54.030.F` - Parking space design if spaces are provided - All parking spaces provided on the lot, whether or not required, must meet parking space and access standards in `23.54.030`. `SMC 23.54.030` - Minimum stall sizes: - Medium vehicle (typical residential standard): 8 ft x 16 ft. `SMC 23.54.030.A.2` - Large vehicle: 8 ft x 19 ft; Small vehicle: 7.5 ft x 15 ft. `SMC 23.54.030.A.1` `SMC 23.54.030.A.3` - For residential uses: - If five or fewer spaces are provided, each must at least meet the medium vehicle size. `SMC 23.54.030.B.1.a` - For an individual garage serving an individual dwelling unit, the minimum stall size is also medium vehicle. `SMC 23.54.030.B.1.d` - Barrier‑free (accessible) parking must be provided if required by the Seattle Building or Residential Code, and must meet the dimensional standards in `23.54.030.A.4`. `SMC 23.54.030.B` `SMC 23.54.030.A.4` - Structural encroachments into stalls, tandem parking definitions, and special rules for stalls next to lot lines also apply. `SMC 23.54.030.A.5` `SMC 23.54.030.A.6` `SMC 23.54.030.A.7` `SMC 23.54.030.C.1` - EV‑ready infrastructure if new parking is built - When new parking spaces are provided on a lot with a new building, a portion of residential parking must be EV‑ready (raceway and panel capacity, not necessarily chargers installed). `SMC 23.54.030.L` - For private parking for an individual dwelling unit (e.g., a private garage for the ADU or principal unit), at least one space in that garage/carport/area must be EV‑ready. `SMC 23.54.030.L.1.a` - For surface parking serving multiple dwelling units, the first 12 spaces must be EV‑ready when up to 25 spaces are provided; above 25 spaces, 45 percent of all spaces must be EV‑ready. `SMC 23.54.030.L.1.b` - For garages serving multiple dwelling units, 45 percent of spaces must be EV‑ready. `SMC 23.54.030.L.1.c` - At least 20 percent of accessible stalls (minimum two) must be EV‑ready where both accessible and EV‑ready spaces are required. `SMC 23.54.030.L.6` - The Director may reduce EV‑ready requirements if meeting them would trigger certain electrical infrastructure upgrades, per the detailed criteria in `23.54.030.L.4`. `SMC 23.54.030.L.4` - Off‑site parking (if used to meet any other required parking on the lot) - If the project relies on off‑site parking to meet required parking for any use on the lot (not the ADU itself), that off‑site parking must: - Be allowed on the off‑site lot and meet applicable parking standards. `SMC 23.54.025.A.1` `SMC 23.54.025.A.2` - Obtain a permit establishing off‑site parking as a use, with proper applications from both property owners. `SMC 23.54.025.C` - Have a recorded notice with legal descriptions and a diagram of the spaces on both lots. `SMC 23.54.025.D` - Off‑site parking cannot serve more than one use unless authorized as shared parking under `23.54.020.G`. `SMC 23.54.025.B.3` `SMC 23.54.020.G` - Parking for rented/leased multifamily units (if ADU is rented in a multifamily context) - For rented or leased multifamily dwelling units, accessory off‑street parking may not be bundled into the dwelling unit rental agreement; it must be in a separate agreement or addendum, with exceptions for moderate‑income units and units with individual garages functionally part of the dwelling (e.g., townhouses/rowhouses). `SMC 23.42.070.A`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 3/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> The retrieved materials only give a partial picture of ADU-related parking rules; they clearly show some important exemptions and interactions but not a single consolidated “full set.” Below is what can be stated from the available documents, and where the gaps are. --- ### 1. Brief Issue Overview Seattle’s Land Use Code (SMC Title 23) sets general off‑street parking quantity, design, and street‑improvement rules, and then carves out specific exemptions for accessory dwelling units (ADUs). For ADUs, SMC explicitly prohibits requiring new public street improvements as a permit condition, and excludes ADUs from dwelling‑unit counts used to calculate such improvements. [Source 10] SMC also states that no minimum off‑street parking is required for accessory dwelling units. [Source 6] Director’s Rules then interpret how parking standards (including mechanical lifts and bicycle parking) apply to required parking generally, but they do not add any new vehicle‑parking requirement specifically for ADUs in the retrieved text. [Source 16] [Source 24] --- ### 2. Side-by-Side Comparison | Topic | Agency | Requirement | Citation | |-------|--------|-------------|----------| | Off‑street parking minimums for ADUs | SMC | “No parking is required for accessory dwelling units.” | [Source 6] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.799 | | Off‑street parking minimums for principal dwelling units on small lots | SMC | No parking required for principal dwelling units on lots in any residential zone that are less than 3,000 sq ft or less than 30 ft in width where access is permitted through a required yard or setback abutting a street. | [Source 6] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.799 | | Interaction with other minimum‑parking provisions | SMC | If more than one provision in Table B for 23.54.015 applies, the provision requiring the least amount of minimum parking applies. | [Source 6] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.799 | | ADUs and public street improvements | SMC | “No public street improvements, other than public street improvements required by state or federal law, shall be required as a condition of permitting accessory dwelling units… For purposes of calculating required street improvements in this Chapter 23.53, accessory dwelling units shall be excluded from dwelling unit counts.” | [Source 10] SMC — smc_chapters, § 23.53.003, p.759 | | Scope of other applicable regulations | SMC | Chapter 23.53 notes that other regulations, including the Seattle Fire Code, may apply to development proposals (not ADU‑specific, but applies to projects generally). | [Source 10] SMC — smc_chapters, § 23.53.002, p.759 | | Location of parking in certain residential zones | SMC | In NR1, NR2, NR3 zones, limited parking may be located in certain yards if on‑street parking is restricted; Director may authorize a wider curb cut than 23.54.030 if necessary for access. (Applies to parking layout if you choose to provide spaces; not ADU‑specific.) | [Source 11] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.172 | | Parking space size and configuration (general) | SMC | Sets minimum vehicle clearance (6 ft 9 in) for certain garages and detailed size/striping standards for residential parking spaces (e.g., medium‑space requirements when more than five spaces are provided). These apply to any provided off‑street parking, including on lots with ADUs. | [Source 7] [Source 9] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.831 | | Parking quantity and drive‑in lanes (general) | SMC | Identifies that quantity of parking required, minimum and maximum parking limits, and minimum and maximum number of drive‑in lanes are regulated in the Land Use Code (cross‑reference to 23.54.015 and .020). | [Source 13] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.100 | | Mechanical parking lifts for required off‑street parking | DIR | Director’s Rule 8‑2003 interprets how mechanical parking lifts can be used to provide required off‑street parking under SMC 23.54.020 and 23.54.030, including how tandem spaces are counted and when minimum size requirements do not apply. (Applies only if you are using lifts to meet any required parking; ADUs themselves have no minimum.) | [Source 18] [Source 24] DIR — seattle_dir_rules, § 8-2003, p.1 | | Bicycle parking performance standards (general) | DIR | Director’s Rule 6‑2020 clarifies performance standards for required bicycle parking under SMC 23.54.015.K; it governs design and placement of required bike parking on private property. (Not ADU‑specific; applies if your project triggers bicycle‑parking requirements.) | [Source 16] [Source 21] [Source 22] DIR — seattle_dir_rules, § 6-2020, p.1–2 | --- ### 3. Friction Summary | # | Agencies | Friction Type | Winner | Risk Note | |---|----------|---------------|--------|-----------| | 1 | SMC vs DIR (mechanical lifts) | Type II — Gap/Silence (re ADUs) | SMC (higher than DIR) | SMC removes any minimum parking requirement for ADUs, so DIR’s mechanical‑lift rule only matters if you voluntarily provide or must meet other required parking on the lot. | | 2 | SMC vs DIR (bicycle parking) | Type II — Gap/Silence (re ADUs) | SMC | SMC 23.54.015.K sets the binding bicycle‑parking standard; DIR 6‑2020 only interprets it and does not impose additional ADU‑specific vehicle‑parking obligations. | --- ### 4. Key Takeaways - You cannot be required to provide off‑street vehicle parking spaces solely because you are permitting an ADU; SMC explicitly states “no parking is required for accessory dwelling units.” [Source 6] - The City also cannot require new public street improvements (curb, sidewalk, etc.) as a condition of permitting an ADU, and ADUs are excluded from the dwelling‑unit counts used to calculate such improvements under Chapter 23.53. [Source 10] - If you voluntarily provide or already have off‑street parking, it must still comply with the general design and dimensional standards in SMC 23.54.030 (e.g., space size, clearance, access), and any use of mechanical lifts must follow Director’s Rule 8‑2003. > A developer permitting an Accessory Dwelling Unit (ADU) in Seattle faces a somewhat counterintuitive situation: > There is no requirement to provide parking—but a full set of rules still governs parking if it exists, is modified, or is created. Here is the complete, overlapping set of parking-related obligations—state + local—organized by what actually triggers requirements. --- # 1) Baseline rule: No parking requirement for ADUs Seattle's current code is explicit: * ADUs are exempt from parking requirements * Parking is listed among standards that "do not apply" to ADUs ([Seattle][1]) 👉 This means: * You can permit an ADU with zero off-street parking * This applies citywide, not just near transit This aligns with Washington State preemption (RCW 36.70A.680/.681), which limits or prohibits ADU parking mandates—but Seattle goes further by eliminating them entirely. --- # 2) If parking already exists: replacement / retention constraints Even though no parking is required, the code still regulates existing parking on the lot: * Existing required parking for the primary dwelling may need to be: * Maintained, or * Relocated in a compliant location if altered This comes from the general rule that: * Parking supply tied to existing development is governed by SMC Title 23 (Land Use Code) 👉 Practical effect: * Converting a garage to an ADU or living space can trigger: * A requirement to replace that parking elsewhere on-site, depending on how it was originally required --- # 3) If you add or keep off-street parking: full SMC design + permitting rules apply Any parking that is provided voluntarily must comply with the entire Seattle parking regulatory framework: ## A. Land Use Code (SMC 23.54) Controls: * Stall dimensions * Driveway geometry * Maneuvering space * Location on lot (front/side/rear yard limits) ([Seattle][2]) ## B. Neighborhood residential siting rules (SMC 23.44) Typical constraints: * No parking within front setback zones * Limits on parking in side yards facing streets * Restrictions on number of outdoor vehicles ([Seattle][3]) ## C. Permitting triggers Creating parking may require: * Construction or grading permits * A curb cut permit for driveway access * SEPA review for large parking areas ([Seattle][2]) 👉 Key point: > Even though parking is optional, it is heavily regulated if you choose to include it. --- # 4) Accessibility requirements (Building Code) If parking is provided: * The Seattle Building Code (SBC Chapter 11) requires: * Accessible parking where applicable * ADA-compliant design for certain project types ([Seattle][2]) 👉 For most small ADU projects this is limited, but it still applies if thresholds are met. --- # 5) Environmental and site constraints Parking areas must also comply with: * Grading Code (SMC 22.170) — if excavation or paving is involved * Environmentally Critical Areas (SMC 25.09) — wetlands, slopes, etc. ([Seattle][2]) 👉 These can restrict *where* parking can be placed—even if otherwise allowed. --- # 6) Bicycle parking: generally not required for ADUs Unlike multifamily buildings: * Seattle's bicycle parking requirements (SMC 23.54.015) apply to new multifamily and commercial development * ADUs (as accessory to a house) are not typically subject to minimum bike parking requirements The city's framework shows: * Bike parking mandates apply to "new construction and redevelopment" by use category (primarily multifamily/commercial) ([Seattle][4]) 👉 In practice: * A standalone ADU permit does not trigger required bike parking --- # 7) What is *not* required (important negatives) For ADUs in Seattle, a developer does NOT need to: * Provide any minimum number of car parking spaces * Provide bike parking * Meet transit-based parking thresholds * Perform parking studies or demand analysis This is unusual compared to: * Middle housing (state caps still apply) * Multifamily (bike parking required) --- # 8) Putting it all together (full regulatory stack) ### Required * Nothing, in terms of minimum parking supply ### Conditionally required (if triggered) * Replacement of existing required parking * Compliance with: * SMC 23.54 (design + layout) * SMC 23.44 (location rules) * SBC accessibility standards * Grading / environmental codes * Permits for: * Driveways / curb cuts * Paving / construction ### Not required * Off-street parking minimums * Bicycle parking --- # Bottom line For an ADU in Seattle: * Parking is legally optional (a major departure from traditional zoning) * But the moment parking is touched, altered, or added, it becomes subject to a dense web of technical regulations 👉 The real compliance burden isn't *how much parking you must build*—it's: > how carefully you must design and permit any parking you choose to include or modify. ### Q021. In commercial zones, if a surface parking area that is nonconforming due to a lack of required landscaping is expanded by ten percent or more, what specific Land Use Code sections dictate the standards it must be screened and landscaped according to?

**Reference answer:** "In commercial zones, surface parking areas that are nonconforming due to lack of required landscaping and are proposed to be expanded by ten percent or more in number of parking spaces or in area are required to be screened and landscaped according to the standards of Section 23.47A.016, or in the SM zone, according to Section 23.48.055, to the extent feasible as determined by the Director."

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> In commercial zones, when a nonconforming surface parking area (nonconforming due to lack of required landscaping) is expanded by 10 percent or more in number of spaces or area, it must be screened and landscaped according to: - `SMC 23.47A.016` (Landscaping and screening standards for commercial zones), or - `SMC 23.48.055` (Landscaping and screening standards in Seattle Mixed zones) if the lot is in an SM zone, to the extent feasible as determined by the Director. `[SMC 23.42.128]`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> In commercial zones, a nonconforming surface parking area that lacks required landscaping and is proposed to be expanded by ten percent or more must be screened and landscaped according to: - SMC 23.47A.016 for commercial zones generally, or - SMC 23.48.055 if the site is in an SM zone, “to the extent feasible as determined by the Director.” [Source 1] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 23 - Land Use Code, p.150

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 1/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> In Seattle's Land Use Code, once a nonconforming surface parking area in a commercial zone is expanded by 10% or more, it loses its "grandfathered" status with respect to landscaping deficiencies and must be brought into compliance with current standards. The code is very specific about *which standards apply*: --- # Required screening and landscaping standards The expanded parking area must comply with: * SMC 23.45.514 — *Parking area screening and landscaping (commercial zones)* * SMC 23.45.518 — *Additional landscaping and screening requirements (including perimeter and interior planting standards)* --- # What this means in practice Those sections require things like: * Perimeter screening (e.g., landscaping or screening between parking and streets/sidewalks) * Interior landscaping within the parking area (planting islands, trees, etc.) * Minimum widths, spacing, and plant types for screening features So if an older lot lacked these features, a ≥10% expansion triggers: > Full compliance with current landscaping and screening standards, not just incremental improvements. --- # Bottom line For a nonconforming surface parking lot in a commercial zone: * Trigger: Expansion ≥ 10% * Result: Must be brought into compliance with * SMC 23.45.514 and * SMC 23.45.518 These sections define the exact screening and landscaping requirements the upgraded parking area must meet.

### Q022. If a development proposal in the Downtown Urban Center is exempt from design review due to Downtown Activation Plan provisions (under subsection 23.41.004.E.3), what specific parking and bicycle parking standards might the Director consider requests for waivers or modifications of?

**Reference answer:** "Waiver or modification of development standard. If a project is exempt from design review according to this subsection 23.41.004.E.3, the Director may consider requests for waivers or modifications of the following development standards in Title 23... Parking space size requirements in subsections 23.54.030.A and 23.54.030.B; Bicycle parking minimum quantity requirements in Table D for 23.54.015".

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> A Downtown Activation Plan–eligible project can request Director waivers/modifications of several specific motor-vehicle and bicycle parking standards. ### Relevant Downtown Activation Plan provision - If a development proposal is exempt from design review under the Downtown Activation Plan (`23.41.004.E.3`), the Director may consider waivers or modifications of certain development standards listed in `23.41.004.E.3.b`, if doing so would increase dwelling units, lodging rooms, or R&D lab floor area. `[SMC 23.41.004]` ### Motor-vehicle parking standards - The Director may consider waivers or modifications of minimum dimensions and slope of vehicle access. `[SMC 23.41.004]` - The Director may consider waivers or modifications of parking space size requirements in `23.54.030.A` and `23.54.030.B`. `[SMC 23.41.004]` - The Director may consider waivers or modifications of minimum dimensions and slope of vehicle access even where those standards functionally control parking layout and access geometry. `[SMC 23.41.004]` > Note: Quantity of parking (minimums/maximums) cannot be departed from under the general departure section `23.41.012.B.14`, but under the Downtown Activation Plan the Director’s waiver authority is limited to the specific dimensional/space-size/access items listed above, not to changing the numeric parking quantity limits. `[SMC 23.41.012]` `[SMC 23.41.004]` ### Bicycle parking standards - The Director may consider waivers or modifications of bicycle parking minimum quantity requirements in Table D for `23.54.015`. `[SMC 23.41.004]` - This authority is expressly limited to minimum quantity; no Downtown Activation Plan provision authorizes waivers of bicycle parking design or dimensional standards outside of what is tied to that quantity table. `[SMC 23.41.004]`

> Even when a Downtown project is exempt from design review under SMC 23.41.004.E.3 (Downtown Activation Plan provisions), it doesn't become free of parking rules. Instead, the exemption shifts discretion to the Director, who can consider waivers or modifications of specific parking and bicycle parking standards that would otherwise apply. Here are the key standards that typically come into play: --- # 1) Off-street vehicle parking standards (SMC 23.54) The Director may consider flexibility on core design and access requirements, including: * Parking space dimensions and configuration (stall width/length, aisle widths, maneuvering requirements) — SMC 23.54.030 * Access and driveway standards (curb cuts, lane widths, ingress/egress layout) — SMC 23.54.030 & 23.54.040 * Location and layout of parking (e.g., structured vs. surface placement, relationship to street frontage) — SMC 23.54.015 / .030 * Quantity limits or minimums where still applicable (though Downtown generally has no minimums, there may be caps or special conditions) 👉 In practice, this allows departures from rigid dimensional or access rules where strict compliance would conflict with activation goals. --- # 2) Bicycle parking standards (SMC 23.54.015) This is often the most important category, since bike parking *is required Downtown*. The Director may consider waivers/modifications to: * Required quantities (short-term and long-term bike parking ratios) * Location requirements (e.g., proximity to entrances, visibility from street) * Design and layout standards (rack type, spacing, vertical vs. horizontal storage, enclosure requirements) * Accessibility and usability standards (clearances, route access, weather protection expectations) 👉 For example, constrained sites or adaptive reuse projects may justify alternative configurations. --- # 3) Screening, landscaping, and frontage-related parking standards Where applicable, the Director may also consider flexibility in: * Screening of parking areas (especially where ground-floor activation is prioritized) * Landscaping requirements tied to parking areas * Street-facing parking design constraints These typically derive from: * Downtown zone standards in SMC Chapter 23.49, and * General parking provisions in SMC 23.54 --- # 4) Purpose of these waivers The Downtown Activation Plan provisions are intended to: * Encourage active street-level uses * Reduce barriers from rigid parking design rules * Enable adaptive reuse and infill on constrained sites So the Director's authority is not unlimited—it's exercised to: > Remove specific parking or bicycle parking requirements that would otherwise prevent activation-oriented design. --- # Bottom line If a Downtown project is exempt from design review under SMC 23.41.004.E.3, the Director may consider waivers or modifications of: * SMC 23.54 off-street parking standards (dimensions, access, layout, and related requirements) * SMC 23.54.015 bicycle parking standards (quantities, location, and design) * Related screening/landscaping and frontage rules tied to parking --- ## Key insight Design review normally serves as the mechanism to grant flexibility. This exemption replaces that process with targeted administrative discretion, focused heavily on parking and bike parking constraints that could otherwise block Downtown activation.

### Q023. Under WAC 365-196-875, what are the two specific conditions under which the provisions limiting local governments from requiring off-street parking for middle housing do not apply?

**Reference answer:** "The provisions of subsections (2) and (3) of this section do not apply: (a) To those areas where the department has certified a local government empirical study, prepared by a certified credentialed transportation or land use expert, meeting the requirements of RCW 36.70A.681 (2)(b)(i) or 36.70A.635 (7)(a);or (b) To portions of cities within a one-mile radius of a commercial airport in Washington with at least 9,000,000 annual enplanements.",

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> The two specific conditions are: 1. Areas covered by a certified empirical parking study – where the department has certified a local government empirical study prepared by a credentialed transportation or land use expert under `RCW 36.70A.635(7)(a)` or `RCW 36.70A.681(2)(b)(i)`. `[WAC 365-196-875]` 2. Areas within one mile of a large commercial airport – portions of cities within a one-mile radius of a commercial airport in Washington with at least 9,000,000 annual enplanements. `[WAC 365-196-875]`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> Under WAC 365-196-875, the limits on local governments’ ability to require off‑street parking for middle housing do not apply in these two situations: 1. Certified empirical parking study – where the Department of Commerce has certified an empirical study, prepared by a credentialed transportation or land use expert, showing that applying the state parking limits would be significantly less safe for drivers, passengers, pedestrians, or bicyclists than applying the jurisdiction’s own parking requirements for the same location and number of detached houses.[Source 7] WAC — wac_chapters, § 365-196, p.163; [Source 12] WAC — wac_chapters, § 365-199-090, p.12 2. Areas near major commercial airports – portions of cities within a one‑mile radius of a commercial airport in Washington with at least 9,000,000 annual enplanements.[Source 7] WAC — wac_chapters, § 365-196, p.163

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 0/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> Under WAC 365-196-875, the limits on requiring off-street parking for middle housing (like duplexes and fourplexes) are not absolute. The rule identifies two specific exceptions where those limits do *not* apply: --- # 1) Documented health, safety, or infrastructure constraint A local government may impose parking requirements if it makes a specific, evidence-based finding that the limits would: * Cause a significant adverse impact to public health or safety, or * Conflict with available water, sewer, or stormwater infrastructure capacity 👉 This isn't discretionary in a general sense—the jurisdiction must: * Conduct analysis, and * Adopt findings supporting the need for the exception --- # 2) Areas at risk of displacement (equity / anti-displacement planning) The limits also don't apply where a jurisdiction has: * Identified an area as being at higher risk of displacement, and * Adopted policies (e.g., in a comprehensive plan) addressing that risk 👉 In those areas, a city may require parking if doing so is tied to anti-displacement strategies. --- # Bottom line The state's parking limits for middle housing can be overridden only when: 1. Health/safety or infrastructure impacts are formally demonstrated, or 2. The area is designated as displacement-risk and the requirement is part of an anti-displacement approach Outside of those two conditions, jurisdictions like Seattle must follow the state limits and cannot impose stricter parking minimums.

### Q024. If an office and a residential use share required off-street parking under the cooperative/shared parking rules, what is the maximum percentage the residential parking can be reduced, and what is the limiting condition for this reduction?

**Reference answer:** "If an office and a residential use share required off-street parking, the required parking for the residential use may be reduced by 50 percent, provided that the reduction does not exceed the minimum required parking for the office use.",

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> The residential parking can be reduced by a maximum of 50 percent when an office and a residential use share required off-street parking. The reduction is limited by the condition that it may not exceed the minimum required parking for the office use. `SMC 23.54.020.G.2.d`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> The SMC shared/cooperative parking provisions allow a residential use sharing required off‑street parking with an office use to reduce its required residential parking by up to 50%. The limiting condition is that the reduction cannot exceed the minimum required parking for the office use. [Source 2] [Source 3]

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 2/5, Faithfulness 2/5

> Under Seattle's cooperative/shared parking provisions in the Land Use Code (SMC 23.54), residential parking can be reduced—but only to a limited extent and under a specific constraint. --- # Maximum reduction * The residential portion of the required parking may be reduced by up to 50% when shared with a nonresidential use such as an office. --- # Limiting condition That reduction is only allowed to the extent that the peak parking demands do not overlap. In other words: > The shared parking analysis must demonstrate that residential demand (typically evenings/nights) and office demand (typically daytime) occur at different times, so the same spaces can serve both uses without conflict. --- # What this means in practice * A developer can't automatically take the full 50% reduction * They must show (often via a parking study or standard assumptions) that: * Combined peak demand fits within the reduced supply, and * There is no significant temporal overlap in usage --- # Bottom line * Maximum reduction: 50% of required residential parking * Condition: Only allowed where shared-use timing demonstrates non-overlapping peak demand between residential and office uses If the timing overlap is too great, the allowed reduction would be less than 50% (or none at all).

A fence or wall `[SMC 23.45.536.D.1.c]` 4. Landscaped areas, including bioretention facilities or landscaped berms `[SMC 23.45.536.D.1.d]`

A view-obscuring fence at least 3 feet high. 2. A view-obscuring wall at least 3 feet high. 3. A solid evergreen hedge at least 3 feet high. 4. A landscaped berm or bioretention facility where the top of the vegetation is at least 3 feet above the adjacent grade. ### Q027. Are existing parking spaces required to be modified if they do not meet the new state dimension or size requirements by June 6, 2024?

**Reference answer:** "Existing parking spaces that do not conform to the requirements of this section by June 6, 2024, are not required to be modified or resized, except for compliance with the Americans with disabilities act."

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> No. Existing parking spaces that do not conform to the new state requirements as of June 6, 2024 are not required to be modified or resized, except as needed to comply with the Americans with Disabilities Act. Existing paved parking lots also are not required to change the size of existing parking spaces during resurfacing if doing so would be more costly or require significant reconfiguration of parking space locations. `[RCW 36.70A.622]`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 3/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> Based on the available IBC_WA materials, there is no provision that generally requires all existing parking spaces to be retroactively modified to meet new dimensional or size standards by June 6, 2024; the cited sections apply prospectively to new construction and to alterations. Section 429.1 states that the electric vehicle charging infrastructure provisions “shall apply to the construction of new buildings and accessory structures, including parking lots and parking garages,” indicating they are triggered by new work, not by the mere passage of time or a date threshold.[Source 11] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § IBC International Building Code, p.55. For energy code requirements, Section R503.1 similarly provides that “alterations to any building or structure shall comply with the requirements of the code for new construction,” but it does not require unaltered portions of existing buildings to be upgraded solely because the code changed.[Source 3] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § IBC International Energy Code Residential, p.60. The materials also expressly exempt existing buildings from certain new “accessible means of egress” requirements, again indicating a non-retroactive approach unless work is undertaken.[Source 4] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § IBC International Building Code, p.117. Whether any separate statute or local ordinance imposes a June 6, 2024 retrofit deadline “could not be determined from the available materials.”

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 2/5

> No. Under Washington's updated parking standards, existing parking spaces are not required to be modified to meet the new dimensional or size requirements by June 6, 2024. The state rules (as implemented through WAC 365-196-875 and related GMA provisions) are forward-looking, meaning they apply to: * New development, and * Newly constructed or reconfigured parking areas They do not require retrofitting of legally established ("existing" or "nonconforming") parking spaces. --- # What this means in practice * If a parking lot or spaces were lawfully created under prior standards, they can remain as-is * Cities like Seattle cannot force property owners to: * Resize stalls * Re-stripe lots * Reconstruct parking solely to meet new state dimensions --- # When changes *would* be required Existing spaces may need to comply only if triggered by a project, such as: * Redevelopment or major site alterations * Expansion or reconfiguration of parking * Loss of nonconforming status under local code --- # Bottom line * No blanket retrofit requirement by June 6, 2024 * New standards apply prospectively, not retroactively * Existing parking remains legal unless a project triggers compliance

### Q030. What happens if a local city's development regulations regarding accessory dwelling unit parking conflict with the state requirements outlined in RCW 36.70A.698?

**Reference answer:** "Beginning July 1, 2021, the requirements of RCW 36.70A.698: ... (b) Supersede, preempt, and invalidate any local development regulations that conflict with RCW 36.70A.698."

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> State law preempts: conflicting local ADU parking regulations are superseded, invalid, and may not be enforced. - Cities planning under `RCW 36.70A.040` must adopt or amend their development and zoning regulations to incorporate the requirements of `RCW 36.70A.698` by ordinance. If they do not, the state requirements apply directly and supersede, preempt, and invalidate any conflicting local development regulations. `[RCW 36.70A.697]` - Under `RCW 36.70A.698(1)`, cities generally may not require off‑street parking for ADUs within one‑quarter mile of a major transit stop, except where they make an evidence‑based finding that on‑street parking is infeasible due to lack of capacity, physical impediments, or other supported reasons. `[RCW 36.70A.698]` - Beginning July 1, 2021, these state ADU parking limits automatically apply in any city that has not adopted compliant ordinances, and in those cities they supersede, preempt, and invalidate any conflicting local ADU parking regulations. `[RCW 36.70A.697]` - Separately, for jurisdictions subject to `RCW 36.70A.680` and `36.70A.681`, state law also bars requiring off‑street parking for ADUs within one‑half mile of a major transit stop and caps required spaces per unit by lot size, with narrow exceptions; these provisions likewise supersede, preempt, and invalidate conflicting local regulations where a city has not yet adopted compliant ordinances. `[RCW 36.70A.680]` `[RCW 36.70A.681]` - Commerce’s implementing rule confirms that, where state ADU and middle‑housing parking limits conflict with older minimum‑parking statutes, the least restrictive off‑street parking requirement should apply, reinforcing that local codes cannot impose stricter ADU parking than the state maximums. `[WAC 365-196-875]`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> State law expressly preempts conflicting local ADU parking rules, so the RCW standard controls and the local regulation is invalid to the extent of the conflict. RCW 36.70A.698 requires that cities “may not require” off‑street parking for ADUs within one‑quarter mile of a major transit stop (with narrow, evidence‑based exceptions), and RCW 36.70A.697 states that these state requirements “supersede, preempt, and invalidate any local development regulations that conflict” with RCW 36.70A.698. > The least restrictive parking standard governs when `RCW 36.70A.620` conflicts with `RCW 36.70A.635` (middle housing) or `RCW 36.70A.681` (ADUs). ### Applicable precedence rule - Commerce’s implementing rule states that when the number of off‑street parking spaces required by `RCW 36.70A.620` conflicts with `RCW 36.70A.635` or `RCW 36.70A.681`, “the least restrictive off-street parking requirement should apply.” `[WAC 365-196-875]` ### Comparison | Topic | Rule set involved | Direction on conflict / precedence | Source | |--------------------------------|-------------------------------------------|----------------------------------------------------------------|--------| | Minimum residential parking vs. middle housing / ADU parking | `RCW 36.70A.620` vs. `RCW 36.70A.635` and `RCW 36.70A.681` | Least restrictive off‑street parking requirement should apply when these statutes conflict | `WAC 365-196-875` | So, if a project qualifies under both sets of provisions and they differ, the jurisdiction must apply whichever parking requirement is less restrictive.

[Source 3] RCW 36.70A.635 (middle housing) now directly limits local parking by prohibiting off‑street parking requirements within one‑half mile of a major transit stop and capping parking at one space per unit on lots ≤ 6,000 sq. ft. [Source 14] RCW 36.70A.698 separately prohibits cities from requiring off‑street parking for ADUs within one‑quarter mile of a major transit stop, with only narrow, evidence‑based exceptions. [Source 7] In any apparent conflict, you should treat the specific middle‑housing and ADU parking provisions (and related newer parking statutes such as RCW 36.70A.817 and 36.70A.622) as the controlling rules, not the decodified RCW 36.70A.620. ### Q034. Since Seattle has no legal minimum size for parking spaces, can I stripe 6‐foot‐long stalls for my apartment building?

**Reference answer:** The premise is false. SMC 23.54.030 does set minimum stall sizes. The system should correct the premise and answer: small‐vehicle stalls must be at least 7.5 ft x 15 ft (Title 23, p.830); medium‐vehicle 8 ft x 16 ft; large‐vehicle 8 ft x 19 ft (Title 23, p.829). It should clearly say 6‐foot‐long stalls do not comply.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> Warning: Premise check: Your question states "Seattle has no legal minimum size for parking spaces". However, SMC 23.54.030 establishes minimum dimensions for all off‑street parking spaces, including specific minimum lengths and widths by stall type. `SMC 23.54.030` To answer your actual question: you cannot legally stripe 6‑foot‑long stalls for required off‑street parking in Seattle. ### Minimum stall dimensions - All off‑street parking spaces, whether required or not, must meet the standards of `SMC 23.54.030`. `SMC 23.54.030` - Minimum stall sizes: - Large vehicle: 8 ft wide x 19 ft long. `SMC 23.54.030.A.1` - Medium vehicle: 8 ft wide x 16 ft long. `SMC 23.54.030.A.2` - Small vehicle: 7.5 ft wide x 15 ft long. `SMC 23.54.030.A.3` - Barrier‑free (accessible) stalls must be at least 8 ft wide with a 5–8 ft access aisle and at least 19 ft long for at least one stall. `SMC 23.54.030.A.4` ### Residential parking mix - For residential uses with five or fewer spaces, each stall must be at least medium‑size (8 ft x 16 ft). `SMC 23.54.030.B.1.a` - For more than five residential spaces, at least 60% must be medium stalls; the remaining 40% may be any allowed size (small, medium, or large), but even small stalls must be 15 ft long. `SMC 23.54.030.B.1.b` `SMC 23.54.030.A.3` Because the shortest legal stall type (small) must be 15 feet long, 6‑foot‑long stalls are not permitted for apartment parking under the Seattle Land Use Code. `SMC 23.54.030.A.3`

### Q035. Because state law now bans all off‐street parking requirements for middle housing everywhere, Seattle can't require any parking for a fourplex, right?

**Reference answer:** Misleading: state law does not ban all requirements; it sets maximums and a no‐parking zone near transit. WAC 365‐196‐875(3) says cities may not require any off‐street parking for middle housing within 1⁄2 mile of a major transit stop, and outside that area may not require more than 1–2 spaces per unit depending on lot size

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> Warning: Premise check: Your question states "state law now bans all off‐street parking requirements for middle housing everywhere". However, RCW 36.70A.817 establishes ## RCW 36.. To answer your actual question: Seattle generally cannot require off‑street parking for a fourplex within one‑half mile walking distance of a major transit stop, but may require limited parking elsewhere, subject to state caps and exceptions. ### Core rule for middle housing - Cities subject to the middle‑housing mandate (which includes Seattle) shall not require off‑street parking for middle housing within one‑half mile walking distance of a major transit stop. `RCW 36.70A.630(6)(d)` - Outside that half‑mile area, such cities may require parking, but no more than one space per unit on lots ≤ 6,000 sq ft and no more than two spaces per unit on lots > 6,000 sq ft, before any zero‑lot‑line splits. `RCW 36.70A.630(6)(e)` `RCW 36.70A.630(6)(f)` ### When Seattle could exceed or avoid these limits - The no‑parking and cap rules above do not apply if the city obtains Commerce certification of an empirical safety study showing that the state parking limits would be “significantly less safe” than the city’s own requirements for the same location and number of detached houses. `RCW 36.70A.630(7)(a)` - The same no‑parking and cap rules also do not apply in any portion of the city within one mile of a commercial airport with ≥ 9,000,000 annual enplanements. `RCW 36.70A.630(7)(b)` ### Interaction with other state parking provisions - The general residential‑parking standards in `RCW 36.70A.622` (e.g., tandem counting, max stall size, tree‑retention infeasibility rule) apply to all residential development, including middle housing, unless a more specific statute controls. `RCW 36.70A.622(1)` - Commerce’s parking guidance directs that where `RCW 36.70A.620`/`36.70A.622` conflict with the middle‑housing and ADU parking limits, the least restrictive off‑street parking requirement should apply. `WAC 365-196-875(5)` (implementing `RCW 36.70A.622`) ### Bottom line for a Seattle fourplex - If the fourplex lot is within ½‑mile walking distance of a major transit stop, Seattle may not require any off‑street parking for that middle‑housing project, unless it has a Commerce‑certified empirical safety study for that area or the site is within the one‑mile airport exception. `RCW 36.70A.630(6)(d)` `RCW 36.70A.630(7)` - If the lot is outside that ½‑mile area and not in an exempted area, Seattle can require some parking, but is capped at the per‑unit limits above; state law does not impose a blanket ban on all off‑street parking requirements for middle housing. `RCW 36.70A.630(6)(e)` `RCW 36.70A.630(6)(f)`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> State law does not ban all off‑street parking requirements for middle housing in all situations; it sets specific caps and transit‑area prohibitions, and Seattle’s code already reflects some of these but could still be constrained where it is stricter than RCW. For middle housing, cities must: (1) not require any off‑street parking within ½‑mile walking distance of a major transit stop, and (2) outside that area, may not require more than 1 space per unit on lots ≤ 6,000 sq ft or more than 2 spaces per unit on lots > 6,000 sq ft, unless an approved safety study justifies more. ### Q036. If a Seattle fourplex is 0.49 miles from a major transit stop by road but 0.51 miles by sidewalk, does the state '1⁄2‐mile walking distance' parking rule apply?

**Reference answer:** The corpus defines the 1⁄2‐mile walking distance rule (no required off‐street parking for middle housing within 1⁄2 mile of a major transit stop) (WAC 365‐196‐875(3)(a), p.163), but does not specify how to measure that distance (road vs. sidewalk vs. straight‐line). The system should explain that the rule exists, but the measurement method is not defined in the retrieved text, so interpretation is ambiguous and would depend on local or Commerce guidance not in the corpus.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 1/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> The state ½‑mile walking distance parking limits do apply if the fourplex is within ½ mile by any allowed measurement method (road/paths/sidewalks or direct line), even if one sidewalk route is 0.51 miles. ### How “walking distance” is measured - For applying state residential parking limits, walking distance to a transit stop may be measured either: - along roads, paths, and sidewalks, or - by direct distance between two points, as long as constraints on walkability (terrain, water, missing pedestrian routes, barriers) are accounted for and documented. `[WAC 365-196-875(1)(c)]` ### Applying that to your example - If any reasonable walking path (by road, path, or sidewalk) from the fourplex to the major transit stop is ≤ 0.50 miles, or if the direct point‑to‑point distance is ≤ 0.50 miles after accounting for barriers, then the site is considered within ½‑mile walking distance, and the state ADU/middle‑housing parking caps and “no parking within ½ mile of major transit stop” rules apply. `[WAC 365-196-875(1)(c)]` `[WAC 365-196-880(2)(k)]` `[RCW 36.70A.680(2)(a)(i)]` - If all compliant measurement methods (road/paths/sidewalks and direct distance, with barriers considered) are > 0.50 miles, then the parcel is not within the ½‑mile area and the special state parking limits for proximity to major transit do not apply. `[WAC 365-196-875(1)(c)]` ### Key nuance for your 0.49 vs 0.51 miles - Because the rule does not require using sidewalks only, and explicitly allows roads/paths or direct distance, a 0.49‑mile road route is sufficient to qualify the site as within ½‑mile walking distance, even if a particular sidewalk route is 0.51 miles. `[WAC 365-196-875(1)(c)`]
