### Q037. Is a three‐unit townhouse project in Seattle always treated as 'middle housing' for the state parking caps, or can the City classify it differently for parking?

**Reference answer:** The system can say that middle housing includes multiplex forms (e.g., duplexes, triplexes, fourplexes) under RCW 36.70A.030(26) and that the state parking caps apply to middle housing, but it must note that whether a specific townhouse configuration is legally "middle housing" is not fully determinable from the retrieved text and may require consulting the full RCW definition.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> A three‑unit townhouse project in Seattle is not always treated as “middle housing” for state parking caps; it only gets the state caps if it meets the statutory definition of middle housing, and Seattle cannot avoid those caps by re‑labeling a qualifying project. ### Middle housing definition - “Middle housing” is defined in `RCW 36.70A.030(26)` (not in provided text), but `RCW 36.70A.536` and `RCW 36.70A.630` show how it is applied: it includes “middle housing types” such as duplexes, triplexes, quadplexes, sixplexes, stacked flats, townhouses, and courtyard apartments on parcels that permit single‑family residences. `[RCW 36.70A.536]` `[RCW 36.70A.630]` - Cities subject to `RCW 36.70A.630` must allow at least six of nine middle‑housing types and apply no more restrictive standards to middle housing than to detached single‑family residences, while allowing the same permit and environmental review processes. `[RCW 36.70A.630]` ### State parking caps for middle housing - For cities subject to `RCW 36.70A.630`, the state sets maximum off‑street parking that may be required for middle housing: - No off‑street parking may be required within ½ mile walking distance of a major transit stop. `[RCW 36.70A.630(6)(d)]` - On lots ≤ 6,000 sq ft, cities shall not require more than 1 space per unit. `[RCW 36.70A.630(6)(e)]` - On lots > 6,000 sq ft, cities shall not require more than 2 spaces per unit. `[RCW 36.70A.630(6)(f)]` - These caps can only be overridden if Commerce certifies an empirical safety study, or in areas within 1 mile of a large commercial airport; otherwise they are binding. `[RCW 36.70A.630(7)]` - Commerce’s parking rule confirms that for middle housing, cities “shall not” require more than these amounts and that these caps are to be treated as maximums that may be reduced. `[WAC 365-196-875(3)]` `[WAC 365-196-875(6)]` (implements `RCW 36.70A.635` / `RCW 36.70A.681` by analogy) ### Can Seattle re‑classify a 3‑unit townhouse? - Seattle’s local code treats townhouses as a distinct residential use and sets its own minimum parking (e.g., 1 space per dwelling unit for townhouse and rowhouse developments in the citywide table). `[SMC 23.54.015 (Table D, D.4)]` - However, state law requires that middle housing not be held to more restrictive standards than detached single‑family residences and that the state parking caps apply to “middle housing” as defined in state law, not as locally labeled. `[RCW 36.70A.630(6)(b)]` - Commerce’s parking rule explicitly states that where the number of off‑street spaces required by `RCW 36.70A.620` conflicts with the middle‑housing parking limits, “the least restrictive off‑street parking requirement should apply.” `[WAC 365-196-875(5)]` (implementing `RCW 36.70A.817` / `RCW 36.70A.635` / `RCW 36.70A.681`) - Because RCW provisions preempt inconsistent local regulations, Seattle cannot avoid the state parking caps for a project that meets the statutory definition of middle housing (e.g., a 3‑unit townhouse on a lot that otherwise permits a single‑family residence) by calling it something else in its zoning code. `[RCW 36.70A.630]` `[WAC 365-196-875(5)]` ### When a 3‑unit townhouse is *not* under the middle‑housing caps - The middle‑housing parking caps in `RCW 36.70A.630(6)(d)–(f)` apply only to “middle housing” in the sense used in that section—i.e., middle housing allowed under the state’s middle‑housing mandate in areas where single‑family detached housing is or was allowed. `[RCW 36.70A.630]` - If a 3‑unit townhouse is in a multifamily or mixed‑use zone that is not being regulated as “middle housing” under `RCW 36.70A.630`/`RCW 36.70A.635` (for example, a larger apartment‑zoned corridor), the specific middle‑housing parking caps in `RCW 36.70A.630(6)` do not apply by their terms, and Seattle may apply its general multifamily parking standards (subject to other state‑level parking limits such as `RCW 36.70A.817` for certain project types). `[RCW 36.70A.630]` `[RCW 36.70A.817]` ### Bottom line - If the 3‑unit townhouse is middle housing on a lot that permits single‑family residences in a city subject to `RCW 36.70A.630`, Seattle must honor the state middle‑housing parking caps and cannot classify it differently just to require more parking. `[RCW 36.70A.630(6)]` `[WAC 365-196-875(3)]` `[WAC 365-196-875(5)]` - If the same 3‑unit townhouse is in a pure multifamily/mixed‑use zone outside the state middle‑housing framework, Seattle may apply its own multifamily parking rules (like `SMC 23.54.015`) so long as they do not conflict with other applicable state parking preemptions (e.g., for passive house, modular, mass timber, or affordable projects under `RCW 36.70A.817`). `[RCW 36.70A.817]` `[SMC 23.54.015]`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> A three-unit townhouse project in Seattle is not automatically “middle housing” for state parking caps; it depends on how Seattle has defined “middle housing” and townhouse in its land use code, but the City’s definitions must still be consistent with state middle-housing rules. WAC allows cities to define duplex, triplex, fourplex, fiveplex, and sixplex so long as those definitions are consistent with the statutory definition of “middle housing,” including compatibility in scale, form, and character with single-family houses and containing two or more attached, stacked, or clustered homes. [Source 8] WAC also requires that when a city is “permitting middle housing,” it cannot impose development standards more restrictive than for detached single-family residences and must apply the same permit and environmental review processes, with only limited exceptions. [Source 9] For parking, WAC sets specific caps for accessory dwelling units near transit and provides a certification process for jurisdictions that want to impose higher off‑street parking requirements for middle housing and ADUs based on safety studies, indicating that “middle housing” is a protected category for parking regulation. ### Q038. Given Seattle's RPZ fees and limits, isn't the City effectively discriminating against low‐income drivers by restricting on‐street parking?

**Reference answer:** It does not address discrimination findings or legal challenges. The system should present the fee and eligibility facts and explicitly decline to make normative or legal‐conclusion statements about "discrimination" beyond what the documents say.

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> Warning: Premise check: Your question states "Seattle's RPZ fees and limits effectively discriminate against low-income drivers by restricting on-street parking". However, SMC 11.16.319 establishes ## 11.. State law allows, but does not require, transportation benefit districts that include a large city and impose certain vehicle fees or taxes to create a rebate program of up to 40% for low‑income individuals; any such district must design and administer the program and report to the Legislature after two years. `[RCW 36.73.067]` ### Permit quantity & usage limits - Each eligible household unit is generally limited to no more than four RPZ permits at any one time, or one permit if the dwelling is a small efficiency dwelling unit; certain group‑living uses (adult family homes, assisted living, domestic violence shelters, permitted congregate residences) may receive more, up to one per sleeping room, based on need and on‑street availability. `[SMC 11.16.315.D]` - Each eligible resident may obtain no more than one annual or biennial guest permit per household and no more than 50 single‑day guest permits per year. `[SMC 11.16.315.E]` - A resident RPZ permit must be used within six contiguous city blocks of the household unit address, and a business/institution/employee permit must be used within six contiguous blocks of the business or institution. `[SMC 11.16.315.B.2.b]` - Motorcycles and scooters are authorized to park in an RPZ without an RPZ permit, which functionally removes the fee barrier for those vehicle types. `[SMC 11.16.315.F]` ### Program purpose & establishment criteria - The Director may establish or expand an RPZ when 75% or more of on‑street capacity on ten contiguous blocks (or 20 block faces) is generally occupied and over 35% of parked vehicles are not owned by residents, with community support and a finding that the public interest is served. `[SMC 11.16.317.B]` - Stated benefits of RPZs include increased access for area residents, reduced congestion, improved safety, reduced commuter parking in neighborhoods, and promoting alternative modes of transportation. `[SMC 11.16.317.A.1]` - Seattle is prohibited from installing RPZs in any downtown zone (all zones in Map 1A of Chapter 23.49) due to high demand and competing needs for on‑street parking. `[SMC 11.16.317.A.3]` ### Low‑income considerations in related programs - Free‑floating car share operators must propose a service area and strategies that serve “a diverse customer base, including low‑income customers,” and SDOT must consider whether free‑floating car share is “sufficiently serving low income communities” when deciding how many permits and operators to allow. `[SMC 11.23.160.B.7]` `[SMC 11.23.160.C]` - State law separately authorizes, but does not mandate, rebates up to 40% of certain vehicle fees, taxes, or tolls for low‑income individuals in qualifying transportation benefit districts. `[RCW 36.73.067]` ### What the sources do *not* say - No provided source defines “discrimination” in this context or declares RPZ fees or limits to be discriminatory against low‑income drivers. To answer your actual question: Washington’s new state and Seattle parking rules regulate how much parking can be required or provided, but none of the cited laws or codes state an intent to push poor people out of car ownership; several provisions instead focus on affordability, safety, and equitable impact. ### 1-line summary No provided source says the new residential parking maximums are intended to force low‑income people out of car ownership; the stated legislative purposes are cost reduction, safety, and impact mitigation, not class-based exclusion. ### Legislative intent & stated purposes - The legislature’s findings for the Parking Reform and Modernization Act say that predetermined on-site parking requirements “needlessly drive up the cost of development, particularly housing,” “discourage walking and multimodal transit usage,” and “encourage excessive reliance of automobiles,” and that parking needs should be determined case‑by‑case by applicants, not by one-size-fits-all regulation. `[RCW 36.01.390]` - These findings frame the reform as reducing development costs and car dependence and do not mention any intent to reduce car ownership by low‑income residents or any other income group. `[RCW 36.01.390]` ### Statewide residential parking maximums - For counties, the state now caps minimum parking requirements at no more than 0.5 spaces per multifamily unit and no more than 1 space per single-family home. `[RCW 36.01.397]` - Counties may not require any minimum parking for: residences under 1,200 sq ft, commercial spaces under 3,000 sq ft, affordable housing, senior housing, licensed child care centers, ground-level nonresidential space in mixed-use buildings, and buildings changing from nonresidential to residential or to another commercial use. `[RCW 36.01.397]` - For code cities (which include Seattle), parallel limits apply: no more than 0.5 spaces per multifamily unit, no more than 1 per single-family home, and no more than 2 per 1,000 sq ft of commercial space, with the same list of categories where no minimum parking may be required. `[RCW 35A.21.445]` ### Special treatment of affordable & small housing - “Affordable housing” is explicitly protected from minimum parking requirements for both counties and code cities, meaning jurisdictions cannot require any minimum parking for affordable housing projects. `[RCW 36.01.397]` `[RCW 35A.21.445]` - Residences under 1,200 sq ft (which are often lower-cost units) also cannot be subject to minimum parking requirements by counties or code cities. `[RCW 36.01.397]` `[RCW 35A.21.445]` ### Off-street parking bans for certain residential projects - Separately, cities and counties planning under the Growth Management Act may not require off-street parking as a condition of permitting residential projects that are: - Affordable housing, - Passive house projects, - Modular construction, or - Mass timber construction, unless they obtain and Commerce certifies an empirical safety study showing that applying the parking limits would be “significantly less safe” for drivers, passengers, pedestrians, or bicyclists. `[RCW 36.70A.817]` - These categories (affordable, energy-efficient, and innovative construction types) are treated to reduce regulatory parking burdens, not to restrict car ownership. `[RCW 36.70A.817]` ### Minimum parking design rules (not ownership limits) - Cities and counties must enforce certain flexible design rules for residential parking: they may not require garages or carports to meet minimums, must count tandem spaces, must allow grass block pavers, and may not require spaces larger than 8' x 20' (except ADA). `[RCW 36.70A.620]` `[RCW 36.70A.622]` - Counties and qualifying cities may not require off-street parking where tree-retention compliance would otherwise make a residential project infeasible. `[RCW 36.70A.620]` `[RCW 36.70A.622]` - These provisions regulate how much and what kind of parking can be required, not whether individuals may own cars. ### Q040. What is the definition of a "high-rise building" for fire code purposes?

**Reference answer:** Under WAC 51-50-0200 (amending IBC Section 202), a "high-rise building" is defined as "a building with an occupied floor, located more than 75 feet (22,860 mm) above the lowest level of fire department vehicle access". For the purposes of this definition, the code specifies that "an occupied roof with an occupant load of 50 or more is considered to be an occupied floor"

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 0/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> Not found in provided sources

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> For Washington’s adopted International Building Code (which the International Fire Code relies on), a “high-rise building” is defined as: > “A building with an occupied floor, located more than 75 feet (22,860 mm) above the lowest level of fire department vehicle access.” [Source 1] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § IBC International Building Code, p.22 For this definition, an occupied roof with an occupant load of 50 or more is treated as an “occupied floor,” so a roof meeting that condition more than 75 feet above fire department access makes the building a high-rise. [Source 1] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § IBC International Building Code, p.22

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 3/5, Faithfulness 2/5

> For Seattle, the definition comes from the Seattle Fire Code, which adopts (with local amendments) the International Fire Code. Definition (fire code): A high-rise building is a building with an occupied floor located more than 75 feet above the lowest level of fire department vehicle access. Key points to interpret this correctly: "Occupied floor" → any floor designed for human occupancy (not just mechanical or roof spaces). "75 feet" → measured vertically from fire department access (typically the street or approved access road). "Lowest level of fire department vehicle access" → where fire apparatus can reasonably reach and operate from. Why this matters Once a building meets this threshold, it triggers a stricter set of fire/life safety requirements under the Seattle Fire Code and Seattle Building Code—things like: Fire command center Emergency voice/alarm communication systems Smoke control systems Enhanced standpipe and sprinkler requirements Firefighter access elevators (in newer codes)

### Q041. What is considered as an "exit"?

**Reference answer:** Seattle Municipal Code (SMC) Title 22 The Seattle Housing Code defines an "exit" as "a continuous and unobstructed means of egress from any place in a building, including intervening aisles, doors, doorways, corridors, exterior exit balconies, ramps, stairways, smokeproof enclosures, horizontal exits, exit passageways, exit courts, yards, or any other permitted means of egress to a street, alley or other public way"

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 5/5

> An “exit” is a continuous and unobstructed means of egress from any place in a building to a street, alley, or other public way, and it includes intervening aisles, doors, doorways, corridors, exterior exit balconies, ramps, stairways, smokeproof enclosures, horizontal exits, exit passageways, exit courts, yards, or any other permitted means of egress to a public way. `SMC 22.204.060(B)`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 1/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> The provided materials do not contain a direct definition of “exit.” They do show related concepts in the Washington-amended IBC/IFC Chapter 10 on Means of Egress: - Section 1006.2.1 requires “two exits or exit access doorways” from a space when occupant load or common path of egress travel exceeds table values, implying an “exit” is a component of the means of egress that leads occupants toward a safe discharge point. `[Source 8] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § 1006.2.1, p.121` `[Source 9] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § 1006.2.1, p.112` - Section 1006.3.4 allows “a single exit or access to a single exit” from certain stories or dwelling units if occupant load, travel distance, and other conditions are met, again treating an “exit” as the protected way out from a story or unit to the level of exit discharge or to independent exits. `[Source 4] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § 1006.3.4, p.114` `[Source 5] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § 1006.3.4, p.123` A precise, formal definition (e.g., “that portion of a means of egress separated from other interior spaces…”) could not be determined from the available materials.

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 2/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> Under Seattle's regulatory framework, the definition of an "exit" comes from the Seattle Building Code, which adopts the International Building Code terminology. ### Definition (code-based) An exit is that portion of a means of egress system that is separated from all other interior spaces of a building or structure by fire-resistance-rated construction and opening protectives, and that provides a protected path of travel to the exit discharge. ### Put more plainly Think of the means of egress as three parts: 1. Exit access – how you get to the exit (corridors, rooms) 2. Exit – the protected path (this is what you're asking about) 3. Exit discharge – the path from the exit to the public way (outside) ### What counts as an "exit" in practice Typical examples include: * Interior exit stairways (enclosed, fire-rated stairs) * Interior exit ramps * Exit passageways (protected corridors leading to the outside) * Exterior exit doors at grade (when they directly discharge outside) ### What does *not* count as an exit * Regular corridors or hallways (those are exit access) * Elevators (except very specific, limited cases) * Open stairs that aren't fire-rated enclosures ### Why the distinction matters Labeling something as an "exit" triggers strict requirements—fire-resistance ratings, continuity, protection from smoke/fire, width, and direct discharge. Misclassifying a corridor as an exit is a common design mistake and can cause permitting issues in Seattle. If you want, I can walk through how many exits your building needs based on occupancy and occupant load—that's where this definition becomes critical.

### Q042. How many exits are required from a single story with an occupant load of 500 people?

**Reference answer:** For a space with an occupant load of 500 people, the code requires a minimum of two exits or exit access doorways. Under WAC 51-50-1006.2.1.1 and WAC 51-54A-1006.2.1.1, the requirement for three exits is only triggered when the occupant load reaches "501 to 1,000". Therefore, 500 occupants is the maximum threshold allowed for a space served by two exits before a third is mandated

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 5/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> Two exits are required from a single story with an occupant load of 500 people, because three exits are only triggered when the occupant load is 501 to 1,000 and four exits when it is greater than 1,000. `[WAC 51-50-1006]`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 2/5

> Under the IBC as amended in Washington, a space with an occupant load up to 500 people is not required to have three or more exits; the “three or more exits” threshold starts at an occupant load of 501.[Source 2] Section 1006.2.1 requires two exits or exit access doorways once the design occupant load or common path of egress travel distance exceeds the values in Table 1006.2.1, but that table is not included in the provided materials.[Source 8] From the available text, the minimum clearly established requirement for a 500‑person space is two exits or exit access doorways, assuming the Table 1006.2.1 thresholds are exceeded.[Source 2][Source 8]

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 3/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> Under the Seattle Building Code (which follows the International Building Code), the required number of exits is determined by occupant load per story. ### Code requirement For a single story with an occupant load of 500: * More than 500 occupants → minimum of 3 exits ### Key threshold logic (IBC Table 1006.3.1) * 1–500 occupants → 2 exits * 501–1,000 occupants → 3 exits * Over 1,000 → 4 exits ### Important nuance At exactly 500 occupants, you are still within the "not more than 500" category. 👉 So the correct requirement is: * Minimum of 2 exits ### Don't stop there In practice, just meeting the count isn't enough. Seattle reviewers will also look at: * Exit separation distance (they must be spaced apart) * Egress width capacity * Arrangement (no dead-end reliance) * Travel distance limits based on occupancy type If you're designing close to that 500 threshold, it's often smarter to plan for 3 exits anyway—it gives flexibility if occupant load increases during permitting or future use changes.

### Q043. What is the maximum travel distance to an exit in a fully sprinklered office building?

**Reference answer:** In a fully sprinklered building, the maximum exit access travel distance for a Group B (office) occupancy is 300 feet. This distance is permitted when the building is equipped throughout with an automatic sprinkler system installed in accordance with Section 903.3.1.1. If the building were not sprinklered, this maximum travel distance would be reduced to 200 feet

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> In a fully sprinklered office building (Group B), the maximum exit access travel distance to an exit is 300 feet. `[WAC 51-50-10170]`

> Under the Seattle Building Code (based on the International Building Code), maximum travel distance depends on occupancy classification and whether the building is sprinklered. ### For an office building (Group B), fully sprinklered: * Maximum exit access travel distance = 300 feet ### What that means * This is the distance from the most remote point in a space to the nearest exit (or to the entrance of an exit, like an enclosed stair). * It assumes the building is equipped with an NFPA 13-compliant automatic sprinkler system. ### Why it's higher when sprinklered Sprinklers significantly reduce fire growth and smoke spread, which allows: * Longer evacuation paths * More flexibility in floor layout ### Watch-outs in Seattle reviews Even if you're under 300 feet, plans can still get flagged if: * The path is not a true, compliant egress path (e.g., obstructed or unclear) * You exceed common path of egress travel limits earlier in the route * You rely on dead-end corridors beyond allowed limits If you want, I can map out how travel distance, common path, and exit separation all interact—this is where most designs run into issues.

### Q044. What is the minimum fire-resistance rating required for walls enclosing an interior exit stairway?

**Reference answer:** The minimum fire-resistance rating for walls enclosing an interior exit stairway varies based on the building configuration and the specific code being applied. Seattle Municipal Code (SMC) Title 22 According to the Seattle Housing Code (Subchapter IV), the walls of all portions of a stairway enclosure must be constructed with a fire-resistance rating of at least one hour. There are limited exceptions for existing partitions made of sound lath and plaster or for enclosures located entirely within a single dwelling unit. Washington State Building Code (WAC 51-50 / IBC) The minimum requirements can increase depending on the building's structural design: Horizontal Building Separation: For buildings utilizing a horizontal separation allowance, stairway enclosures that pass through the horizontal assembly must have a fire-resistance rating of not less than two hours. Specific Horizontal Exceptions: If the enclosure walls below the horizontal assembly are rated for three hours, the walls extending above the assembly may be reduced to a 1-hour rating, provided the enclosure connects fewer than four stories. Combustible Stairways in Type IA Construction: In specific cases where interior exit stairways of combustible materials are allowed within a Type IA building (below a horizontal separation), the stairway must be enclosed by 3-hour fire-resistance-rated construction. Rooftop Structures: On Group I-2 roofs with heliports, structures enclosing exit stairways must match the rating of the shaft enclosures below

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 2/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> Walls enclosing an interior exit stairway must have a minimum 2‑hour fire‑resistance rating where they pass through the 3‑hour horizontal assembly in a `510.2` horizontal building separation condition. `[WAC 51-50-0510]`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 2/5, Faithfulness 2/5

> For interior exit stairways that must be smokeproof enclosures in high‑rise buildings (serving floors more than 75 feet above the lowest level of fire department vehicle access), the enclosing walls must have at least a 2‑hour fire‑resistance rating, unless an exception applies. One key exception allows the portion of the enclosure walls above a horizontal assembly to be reduced to a 1‑hour rating if: (1) the walls below that horizontal assembly are at least 3‑hour rated, (2) the enclosure connects fewer than four stories, (3) the opening protectives above the horizontal assembly are at least 1‑hour rated, and (4) the stairway is within a Type IA building serving a building above of Type III, IV, or V construction. [Source 1] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § IBC International Building Code, p.64; [Source 2] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § IBC International Building Code, p.41.

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 2/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> Under the Seattle Building Code (based on the International Building Code), the required fire-resistance rating for interior exit stairway enclosures depends on the number of stories the stair connects. ### Minimum fire-resistance rating: * 2 hours → if the stairway connects 4 or more stories * 1 hour → if the stairway connects 3 stories or fewer ### Important clarifications * The rating applies to the entire enclosure (walls, floor/ceiling assemblies where applicable). * Opening protectives (doors) must also be rated: * Typically 11⁄2-hour doors in 2-hour enclosures * Typically 1-hour doors in 1-hour enclosures * The enclosure must be continuous and uninterrupted from the top to the exit discharge. ### Why this matters Interior exit stairways are the primary life-safety path, so the enclosure is designed to: * Prevent fire/smoke intrusion * Maintain tenable conditions for evacuation * Provide protected firefighter access If you're working on a specific building height or layout, I can help you confirm whether it triggers the 1-hour or 2-hour requirement—and flag common Seattle plan review issues.

### Q045. Are sprinklers required in the elevator shaft of a new residential building?

**Reference answer:** Under WAC 51-50-0903 and WAC 51-54A-0903, an automatic fire sprinkler system is required throughout "all buildings with a Group R fire area". The International Fire Code, as adopted in Washington, provides a specific exception for the "bottom of the elevator hoistway in an enclosed and noncombustible elevator shaft" (WAC 51-54A-0903 / IFC Section 903.2, Exception 2). Outside of this narrow exception for the hoistway bottom, new residential buildings must be protected throughout by an automatic sprinkler system installed in accordance with Section 903.3

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 3/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> - Statewide, automatic sprinklers are generally required throughout all buildings with a Group R fire area, which includes new residential buildings, unless the very limited Group R‑1 recreational cabin exception applies. `903.2.8` [WAC 51-50-008] [WAC 51-54A-0323] - For elevator hoistways specifically in Seattle, sprinklers must be installed at the top and bottom of elevator hoistways where the suspension means are of combustible material (e.g., noncircular elastomeric‑ or polyurethane‑coated steel belts). `2.1` [DR 7-2014] - Those hoistway sprinklers are not required if the elevator suspension means achieve at least an FT‑1 rating when tested per UL 62 and UL 1581. `2.2` [DR 7-2014] - Sprinklers are not required in elevator machine rooms, control rooms, or hoistways of traction elevators if all of the following are met: the space is dedicated to elevator equipment only, protected by smoke detection per NFPA 72, separated by required fire‑resistance‑rated construction, has no unrelated storage, and the elevator machinery is not hydraulic using combustible fluids. `4` [DR 7-2014]

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> For a new residential building, the Washington‑amended IBC/IFC requires an automatic sprinkler system “in new buildings and structures” where triggered by the occupancy‑based provisions of Sections 903.2.1–903.2.12, which include “new Group R occupancies” generally being required to be sprinklered throughout the building.[Source 1] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § IBC International Fire Code, p.91; [Source 12] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § IBC International Building Code, p.60. The code then provides a specific exception for “bottom of the elevator hoistway in an enclosed and noncombustible elevator shaft,” meaning a sprinkler is not required at the pit of such a shaft even when the building is otherwise sprinklered.[Source 1] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § IBC International Fire Code, p.91. Separately, the IBC requires an enclosed elevator lobby with smoke partitions at each floor in sprinklered buildings, but this section addresses smoke control and lobby construction, not sprinklers inside the shaft itself.[Source 2] IBC_WA — ibc_wa_docs, § IBC International Building Code, p.211. From these materials alone, the building’s sprinkler system is generally required for new Group R buildings, but a sprinkler head is not required at the bottom of an enclosed, noncombustible elevator shaft; the code excerpts provided do not clearly state whether sprinklers are required at other locations within the shaft.

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 2/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> Under the Seattle Building Code and the Seattle Fire Code (with reference to NFPA 13): ### Short answer No—sprinklers are generally *not* required inside the elevator shaft (hoistway) of a new residential building. ### How the code treats this * Elevator hoistways are typically non-sprinklered spaces. * Sprinklers are required in associated areas, including: * Elevator machine rooms * Machinery/control spaces * Elevator lobbies (depending on design and code path) ### Important exception (where people get tripped up) Sprinklers may be required at the top and/or bottom of the hoistway *if* certain conditions apply, such as: * The hoistway contains combustible materials * It is not constructed as a fully noncombustible, enclosed shaft * Specific design conditions trigger protection under NFPA 13 When sprinklers are installed in these locations, they trigger additional requirements like: * Shunt trip (power shutdown) coordination with the elevator system * Heat detection to shut down power before sprinkler activation ### Practical takeaway For most new residential buildings in Seattle: * No sprinklers inside the shaft itself * Yes sprinklers in related rooms/spaces * Conditional sprinklers at top/bottom only if design requires it If you're looking at a specific project, this is one of those details worth confirming early—because adding hoistway sprinklers late can create electrical and elevator coordination headaches.

### Q046. Can a single stairway serve as the only means of egress in a three-story building?

**Reference answer:** A single stairway can serve as the sole means of egress in a three-story building under specific conditions defined in the Seattle and Washington codes. SMC Title 22 (Seattle Housing Code) Sprinkler Protection: Housing units on the third floor are permitted to have only one exit if the building's exit ways and common areas are protected by an approved automatic fire-sprinkler system (SMC 22.206.130.C.1.a). Private Stairways: A single exit is allowed if it leads directly to the exterior via an enclosed stairway (1-hour fire-resistance rated) that serves only that specific unit and has no other floor connections (SMC 22.206.130.B.1.b.iii). WAC 51-50 (International Building Code) Occupancy Groups: A single exit is specifically permitted for buildings classified as Group R-3 or R-4 occupancies (WAC 51-50 Table 1006.2.1). Multistory Units: Within individual dwelling units, exit access stairways connecting up to four stories are permitted to be unenclosed under certain area limitations (WAC 51-50-1011.1, Exception 2)

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 3/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> - A single exit (including a single stairway) from a story or occupied roof is permitted where the occupant load, number of dwelling units, and exit access travel distance for the portion of the building served by that exit do not exceed the limits in Tables `1006.3.4(1)` and `1006.3.4(2)`. `[WAC 51-50-008]` - A single exit is also allowed where rooms, areas, or spaces have exits that discharge directly to the exterior at the level of exit discharge. `[WAC 51-50-008]` - Groups R‑3 and R‑4 occupancies are explicitly allowed to have one exit or access to a single exit from the story. `[WAC 51-50-008]` - Individual single‑story or multistory dwelling units may have a single exit from the unit if (1) the unit qualifies as a space with one means of egress under `1006.2.1`, and (2) that exit either discharges directly to the exterior at the level of exit discharge or the exit access outside the unit’s entrance door leads to at least two independent exits. `[WAC 51-50-008]`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> A single stair can sometimes be the only means of egress, but only in limited conditions based on occupancy, occupant load, travel distance, and how/where the stair discharges. IBC_WA allows a single exit or access to a single exit from a story where the occupant load, number of dwelling units, and exit access travel distance are within the limits of Tables 1006.3.4(1) and (2), or where rooms/areas discharge directly to the exterior, for certain parking garages, for Groups R‑3 and R‑4, and for individual dwelling units that meet specific criteria. [Source 18] IBC_WA also allows individual single‑story or multistory dwelling units to have a single exit if they qualify as a “space with one means of egress” and either discharge directly to grade or connect to exit access that leads to at least two independent exits. [Source 7] In Seattle’s housing context, SMC requires occupied floors with housing units above the first floor or where egress does not discharge within 4 feet of grade to have access to at least two unobstructed exits, but allows a single exit for certain small second‑floor or basement housing units, or where the unit’s exit leads directly to the street/yard or via an exterior or one‑hour‑rated enclosed stair serving only that unit. [Source 20] Side-by-Side Comparison | Topic | Agency | Requirement | Citation | |-------|--------|-------------|----------| | Single exit from a story / occupied roof | IBC_WA | A single exit or access to a single exit is permitted from a story or occupied roof if occupant load, number of dwelling units, and exit access travel distance do not exceed Table 1006.3.4(1) or (2), or if spaces discharge directly to exterior, for certain mechanical parking garages, for Groups R‑3 and R‑4, and for qualifying individual dwelling units. | [Source 18] | | Single exit from individual dwelling unit | IBC_WA | Individual single‑story or multistory dwelling units may have a single exit or access to a single exit if they qualify under Section 1006.2.1 as a space with one means of egress and either discharge directly to exterior at level of exit discharge or connect to exit access that leads to at least two independent exits. | [Source 7] | | Number of exits for housing floors | SMC | Occupied floors with one or more housing units above the first floor, or where egress does not discharge within 4 feet vertically of adjacent ground, must have access to at least two unobstructed exits meeting SMC 22.206.130 standards. | [Source 20] | | Single-exit housing units – small upper floors/basements | SMC | Housing units may have a single exit if on a second floor or in a basement with occupant load not more than 10 persons. | [Source 20] | | Single-exit housing units – direct exterior connection | SMC | A housing unit may have a single exit if the exit leads directly to a street, alley, other public right‑of‑way, or yard at ground level, or by an exterior stairway, or by an enclosed one‑hour‑rated stair serving only that unit and not connected to any other floor below. | [Source 20] | Friction Summary | # | Agencies | Friction Type | Winner | Risk Note | |---|----------|---------------|--------|-----------| | 1 | IBC_WA vs SMC | Type III — Standard Differential | IBC_WA sets floor; a single stair serving housing is only allowed for small second‑floor or basement units (≤10 occupants) or where the unit’s exit directly or exclusively connects to the exterior as specified. - For a specific three‑story design, you must check: (1) occupancy group, (2) occupant load and travel distances against IBC_WA Tables 1006.3.4(1)/(2), and (3) whether SMC’s stricter housing‑exit rules apply.

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 2/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> Under the Seattle Building Code (based on the International Building Code), the default answer is: ### Short answer No—one stairway is generally *not* sufficient for a 3-story building. ### Why The code requires at least two exits (or exit access stairways) from: * Each story, and * The building overall, once certain thresholds are exceeded (which most 3-story buildings do). ### When a single stair *is* allowed There are limited exceptions under IBC Section 1006.3.3 (Single Exits / Single Exit Stairways), but they are tightly constrained. A single stair may be permitted only if all applicable conditions are met, including things like: * Low occupant load (typically ≤ 49 per story) * Short travel distances * Limited building height and number of stories * Specific occupancy types (e.g., some residential or business uses) * Sprinkler protection (often required for multi-story cases) ### For a 3-story building specifically A single stair might be allowed *only if*: * It falls within the exception limits (e.g., small residential or business use), and * All criteria in the code table are satisfied (stories, height, occupant load, travel distance, sprinklers, etc.) ### Practical reality in Seattle Most 3-story buildings: * Exceed the occupant load limits, or * Don't meet the strict travel distance constraints, → so they end up needing at least two stairs. ### Bottom line * Default: Minimum 2 stairs required * Exception: Possible with strict compliance to single-exit provisions (rare but doable in small buildings) If you're evaluating a specific project, I can quickly check whether it qualifies for the single-stair exception—this is a high-stakes call that affects layout, cost, and feasibility.

### Q047. Is outdoor burning allowed within Seattle city limits?

**Reference answer:** Outdoor burning is allowed within Seattle city limits provided specific safety and authorization requirements are met. Open burning is only permitted with prior approval from state or local air and water quality management authorities (WAC 51-54A-0307.2.1). Recreational fires are allowed without such prior authorization but must not be conducted within 25 feet of a structure or combustible material (WAC 51-54A-0307.4.2). Additionally, the Seattle Fire Code requires specific permits and fees for outdoor burning activities such as fireworks displays and open flame effects at temporary outdoor assembly events (SMC 22.602.045, Table B)

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 1/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> Outdoor residential and land-clearing burning is not allowed in Seattle because it is a city with a population greater than 10,000, and such burning may not be allowed in any city over that threshold after December 31, 2000. `[WAC 173-425-040]` (implementing `RCW 70A.15.5010`). Within Seattle parks, it is unlawful to ignite or maintain any fire except in a designated stove or fire ring (or a specific approved beach area at Carkeek Park), and all park fires are prohibited between 11:00 p.m. and 6:00 a.m. `[SMC 18.12.270]`. No source in the provided documents authorizes general outdoor burning (e.g., yard debris piles) within Seattle city limits beyond these tightly limited recreational fires in designated park facilities.

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 1/5, Faithfulness 2/5

> Outdoor burning is generally prohibited within Seattle city limits except where specifically allowed by permit or narrow exceptions. The SMC shows that outdoor fireworks use or display is only allowed with a valid Seattle Fire Department Special Events Permit, with specified fees for different types of fireworks activities, indicating that open burning for fireworks is tightly controlled and not generally allowed without permits.[Source 1] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 22 - Buliding and Construction Codes, p.169 Outdoor barbecues, fireplaces, fire tables, or fire pits are allowed only if located outdoors and equipped with a listed gas timer installed downstream of the appliance shutoff valve, which implies that solid-fuel recreational fires are not authorized under the Green Building Standard for new projects using that pathway.[Source 11] DIR — seattle_dir_rules, § 2-2025 - Green Building Standard, p.9 The City’s environmental policies also identify burning of toxics or wastes as a source of particulate air pollution, supporting a policy basis for restricting outdoor burning.[Source 3] SMC — smc_chapters, § Title 25 - Environmental Protection and Historic Preservation, p.74 For any outdoor burning beyond these limited uses (e.g., special events, fireworks, or code-compliant gas appliances), you should assume it is not allowed unless you obtain and comply with a Seattle Fire Department permit.

### Q048. What approvals are required before a building can be occupied after a fire sprinkler system is installed?

**Reference answer:** Before a building can be occupied after the installation of a required fire sprinkler system, it must undergo specific testing and receive formal authorization from both fire and building officials. WAC 51-54A (International Fire Code) Under WAC 51-54A-3306.5, it is unlawful to occupy any portion of a building or structure where an automatic sprinkler system is required until the installation has been "tested and approved". This requirement ensures that the life safety system is fully functional before life-safety risks are introduced by occupants. SMC Title 22 (Seattle Building and Construction Code) The Seattle code establishes that the final authorization for occupancy is the issuance of a Certificate of Occupancy. Final Inspection: Approval for occupancy is granted following a final inspection which determines that the requirements of the building permit—including fire protection systems—have been met. Certificate of Occupancy: This document is defined as the certificate issued by the Director "after final inspection, allowing the building to be occupied". For existing buildings where no certificate has been issued or where a change of occupancy is requested, a building permit is required to obtain this approval. Acceptance Testing: The Seattle Fire Department prescribes fees for "initial and subsequent acceptance tests" of fire and life safety equipment to confirm they comply with the Seattle Fire Code before recommending the building for occupancy

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 2/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> - All required fire and life safety systems, including automatic sprinkler systems (and any associated fire pumps), must be approved, inspected, acceptance tested, and functional in all areas to be occupied before a Temporary Certificate of Occupancy (TCO) can be issued. `DR 19-2017` - In buildings required to be sprinklered throughout, a TCO is only permitted for floors where approved, inspected, and acceptance-tested sprinkler coverage is in service throughout the floors seeking occupancy and all floors below, with valves and supplies arranged so work on unoccupied floors does not require shutting off sprinklers serving occupied floors. `DR 19-2017` - For high-rise buildings, the fire command center must be complete and functional before any TCO is approved, and its functional requirements apply to systems serving the floors seeking TCO. `DR 19-2017` - All required exits, corridors, rated separations, and stairways serving occupied areas must be complete (including exit signs, illumination, handrails, doors, ceiling tiles) with unobstructed egress to the public way; penetrations and shafts must be complete or protected, which is a condition for TCO/occupancy after sprinkler installation. `DR 19-2017` - For buildings required to have a fire alarm system, a TCO will be approved only in areas where the fire alarm system is approved, inspected, acceptance tested, and functional, including activation by sprinkler waterflow alarm devices; the system’s control panel/annunciator must be installed in the required location. `DR 19-2017` - Under state workplace safety rules, automatic sprinkler systems installed to meet a DOSH standard must be properly maintained and acceptance-tested (including flushing, hydrostatic tests, air tests for dry systems, valve operation, drainage tests), and records of acceptance test dates must be kept; these are minimum state requirements that apply in addition to local TCO conditions. `[WAC 296-24-60705]`

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> The core issue is when a building (or part of it) can be occupied relative to completion and approval of the fire sprinkler system, and how that interacts with water system acceptance. IBC_WA sets a baseline that required sprinkler systems must be tested and approved before any occupancy, while Seattle’s Director’s Rule adds detailed conditions for Temporary Certificates of Occupancy (TCO) and SPU governs when the water main connection serving sprinklers can be put into service. [Source 1] [Source 8] [Source 15] [Source 21] Side-by-Side Comparison | Topic | Agency | Requirement | Citation | |-------|--------|-------------|----------| | Occupancy vs. sprinkler completion (general) | IBC_WA | In buildings where an automatic sprinkler system is required, it is unlawful to occupy any portion of the building until the sprinkler installation has been tested and approved, except as allowed under a separate construction/temporary occupancy provision (Section 105.3.4). | [Source 1] | | Water supply for fire protection during construction | IBC_WA | An approved water supply for fire protection (temporary or permanent) must be available as soon as combustible materials arrive, vertical combustible construction begins, or a standpipe is installed. | [Source 6] | | Permit for underground sprinkler supply piping | IBC_WA | A construction permit is required for installation of underground water supply piping serving a water-based fire protection system, from the first control valve to 1 ft above finished floor, with limited exceptions. | [Source 8] | | Fire alarm connection to sprinklers | IBC_WA | When automatic sprinkler systems are installed, they must be connected to the building fire alarm system. | [Source 4] | | TCO – fire/life safety systems in occupied areas | DIR | For a Temporary Certificate of Occupancy, all required fire and life safety systems in the areas to be occupied (including automatic sprinkler systems and any fire pumps) must be approved, inspected, acceptance tested, and functional. | [Source 15] | | TCO – buildings not required to be sprinklered | DIR | In buildings not required by code to be sprinklered, a TCO can be issued without a functioning sprinkler system. | [Source 11] | | Occupancy during renovations – buildings not required to be sprinklered | DIR | Existing buildings not required to have sprinklers may remain occupied during renovations if exiting is not compromised and other life-safety features remain in place; system must be arranged so work can continue in non‑occupied areas without shutting off alarm service to occupied areas. | [Source 19] [Source 13] | | Fire watch for impaired systems | DIR | Fire watch is required for impaired fire alarm, standpipe, and sprinkler systems per Administrative Rule 9.03.17. | [Source 13] | | Storage before sprinkler approval | DIR | In occupancies required to have automatic sprinklers, storage or accumulation of combustible material not associated with construction is prohibited before the sprinkler system is approved. | [Source 12] [Source 16] | | Water main acceptance for fire protection | SPU | After water main construction/repair, SPU will only accept the connection and place it into service once hydrostatic pressure testing, flushing, disinfection, and water quality testing are successfully completed. | [Source 21] | Friction Summary | # | Agencies | Friction Type | Winner | Risk Note | |---|----------|---------------|--------|-----------| | 1 | IBC_WA vs DIR | Type II — Gap/Silence (DIR adds detail) | IBC_WA (higher authority) | DIR’s TCO rules refine but do not contradict the IBC_WA requirement that required sprinklers be tested and approved before occupancy. | | 2 | IBC_WA vs SPU | Type II — Gap/Silence | IBC_WA | IBC_WA governs when occupancy is allowed; [Source 1] [Source 4] [Source 15] - In Seattle, for a TCO you must show all required fire/life safety systems in the TCO area (sprinklers, fire alarm, standpipes, etc.) are inspected, acceptance tested, and functional; only buildings not required to be sprinklered can get a TCO without a working sprinkler system. [Source 11] [Source 15] - SPU must accept and place into service the water main connection (pressure test, flushing, disinfection, water quality) before the sprinkler supply is considered ready for use. [Source 21] - During renovations or partial occupancy, you must maintain active sprinklers and alarms in occupied areas, provide required separations and/or fire watch if systems are impaired, and avoid non-construction combustible storage before sprinkler approval. [Source 12] [Source 14] [Source 17]

**ChatGPT (manual)**: Correctness 3/5, Faithfulness 1/5

> In Seattle, you don't get to occupy a building just because the sprinkler system is installed—the system has to be accepted, tested, and signed off as part of the broader occupancy approval process. ### Governing framework This is driven by the Seattle Fire Code, Seattle Building Code, and NFPA 13. --- ### Required approvals before occupancy #### 1. Sprinkler system acceptance test (SFD) * You must pass a Seattle Fire Department (SFD) acceptance inspection/test * Includes: * Hydrostatic testing * Main drain test * Alarm/flow switch testing * Verification of system coverage and installation 👉 If this fails, occupancy is off the table. --- #### 2. Final sign-off from Seattle Fire Department * After passing testing, SFD provides final approval of the fire protection system * Confirms the system is operational and code-compliant --- #### 3. Approved as-built documentation Typically required to be submitted and accepted: * As-built drawings * Material and equipment data (cut sheets) * Contractor's material and test certificate (NFPA 13) --- #### 4. Integration with fire alarm system * Sprinkler system must be properly tied into the fire alarm system * Requires: * Separate fire alarm acceptance test * Verification that waterflow and supervisory signals transmit correctly --- #### 5. Certificate of Occupancy (CO) or Temporary CO Issued by the City (through SDCI), but only after: * Fire Department approvals are complete * All life safety systems (including sprinklers) are signed off 👉 No CO (or TCO) = no legal occupancy. --- ### Practical reality in Seattle * The sprinkler approval is a gating item—it sits on the critical path to occupancy * Coordination failures (especially alarm + sprinkler integration) are one of the most common causes of delays * Trying to get a Temporary CO without a functioning sprinkler system is rarely approved unless the building isn't required to be sprinklered (which is uncommon for new construction) --- ### Bottom line Before occupancy, you need: * ✅ Passed SFD sprinkler acceptance test * ✅ Final SFD approval * ✅ Documentation submitted and accepted * ✅ Fire alarm integration verified * ✅ CO or TCO issued by the City --- If you're working on a project timeline, I can help you map the exact inspection sequencing—because in Seattle, scheduling SFD at the wrong time can easily cost you weeks.

### Q049. What happens if a fire protection district issues a burning permit for land that is inside Seattle city limits?

**Reference answer:** Unless a specific interlocal agreement grants the district permit-issuing authority for that specific land, a permit issued by a fire protection district for land inside Seattle would likely be invalid, and the permittee would remain subject to the regulations and penalties of the Seattle Fire Code

**PolicyBridge (RAG + graph)**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 4/5

> A fire protection district can only issue a valid burning permit inside Seattle if it has proper statutory authority and any required delegation; otherwise the permit is ineffective and the burner remains subject to state air‑quality and DNR rules and any more‑restrictive Seattle requirements. ### Who may require/issue burn permits - Fire protection districts are political subdivisions/municipal corporations with powers only as conferred by law. `[RCW 52.12.011]` - A fire protection district may assume authority to issue burning permits within its fire protection district by adopting and publishing a resolution; after that, no open fire may be started on land within that fire protection district without a written permit issued by the district, except on forest or cut‑over land where the district may not assume permit authority. `[RCW 52.12.101]` - The commissioners may adopt a resolution authorizing the district to issue fire permits and setting terms and conditions; the resolution becomes effective 10 days after the last of three weekly publications and posting in three public places. `[RCW 52.12.102]` - Any entity (including a fire protection agency) issuing outdoor burning permits under the state clean air act may charge fees to recover administration and enforcement costs. `[RCW 70A.15.5030]` ### Content and effect of a district burn permit - A district burning permit must designate the premises and exact fire location, the nature of material to be burned, the time limit, and may include any special requirements and conditions the issuing officer deems appropriate. `[RCW 52.12.103]` - The permittee must comply with all permit terms, keep a responsible person in charge at all times, keep the fire under control, prevent spread to other property/structures, and extinguish the fire when burning is completed or when directed by district personnel; possession of a permit does not relieve the permittee from liability for damages. `[RCW 52.12.104]` - Violation of any fire‑permit provision or permit condition under chapter 52.12 is a misdemeanor. `[RCW 52.12.105]` `[RCW 52.12.106]` - If a person starts a fire without a required permit, or violates permit provisions and the district must suppress the fire, that person is liable to reimburse the district for suppression costs. `[RCW 52.12.108]` ### Interaction with state outdoor‑burning and DNR authority - “Outdoor burning” is defined as combustion in an open fire or outdoor container without combustion/emission controls. `[RCW 70A.15.5000]` - No person may cause or allow any outdoor fire containing garbage, dead animals, asphalt, petroleum products, paints, rubber, plastics, or other non‑vegetative substances that emit dense smoke or obnoxious odors. `[RCW 70A.15.5010]` - Outdoor burning is prohibited in areas where state or federal ambient air quality standards are exceeded for pollutants from outdoor burning, and in urban growth areas or cities over 10,000 population that are threatened to exceed standards and have reasonably available alternative disposal methods. `[RCW 70A.15.5020]` - Even where otherwise prohibited, outdoor burning may be allowed for managing storm or flood‑related debris, but only if the entity with permitting jurisdiction issues a permit and all conditions in `RCW 70A.15.5010` and `RCW 70A.15.5080(1)` are met. `[RCW 70A.15.5020]` - Fire protection agencies, counties, and conservation districts issuing burning permits must regulate or prohibit outdoor burning as necessary to prevent or abate nuisances, and may not issue permits when any stage of impaired air quality or air‑pollution episode has been declared. `[RCW 70A.15.5030]` - Local air authorities or Ecology may delegate permit issuance/enforcement for certain kinds of burning to fire protection agencies, counties, or conservation districts; this delegation can be withdrawn if the local entity is not effectively administering the program. `[RCW 70A.15.5100]` - For agricultural burning, permits must come from an air authority, Ecology, or a local entity delegated under `RCW 70A.15.5100`; permits must be conditioned to protect air, water, land, and safety, and to minimize air pollution. `[RCW 70A.15.5090]` - DNR must issue and regulate burning permits for specified silvicultural and wildfire‑risk‑reduction activities on lands where it has fire‑protection responsibility; on lands where DNR does not have fire‑protection responsibility, the appropriate fire protection agency is responsible for permitting and regulating outdoor burning (except for certain wildfire‑risk‑reduction permits that remain with DNR). `[RCW 70A.15.5120]` - On lands under DNR protection, a person must have a valid written DNR burning permit to burn flammable material or refuse/waste forest material, unless in areas or circumstances exempted by DNR rule; DNR may impose conditions for life, property, and air quality, and may refuse, revoke, or postpone permits when necessary for safety or to prevent air pollution under chapter 70A.15 RCW. `[RCW 76.04.205]` - In times and localities of unusual fire danger, DNR may suspend any or all burning permits or privileges authorized by `RCW 76.04.205` and may absolutely prohibit the use of fire in such locations. `[RCW 76.04.315]` ### Ecology / WAC outdoor‑burning framework - Chapter `WAC 173-425` applies to all outdoor burning except agricultural and silvicultural burning. `[WAC 173-425-020]` - Ecology or local air authorities may designate fire protection agencies as permitting/enforcing agencies for particular types of burning via agreement. `[WAC 173-425-060]` - A permit is required statewide (under this chapter) for residential burning in most areas, land‑clearing burning, storm/flood‑debris burning, tumbleweed burning in larger counties, weed‑abatement fires, most firefighting‑instruction fires, rare/endangered plant regeneration fires, larger recreational fires, and any other outdoor burning specifically authorized by an air authority or Ecology. `[WAC 173-425-060]` - All permits must include conditions to meet general outdoor‑burning requirements and may add conditions on timing, hours, combustion practices, and weather; permits may also include conditions needed to comply with other laws. `[WAC 173-425-060]` ### DNR small‑fire rules (if DNR has protection responsibility) - On lands protected by DNR, outdoor fire is prohibited unless either a written DNR permit is obtained and DNR rules are followed, or the burning meets the small‑fire exceptions in `WAC 332-24-205` and `WAC 332-24-211`. `[WAC 332-24-201]` - General DNR rules require, among other things: DNR may restrict/refuse/revoke burning due to adverse fire weather or air‑quality concerns; no burning in nonattainment areas except for limited purposes; no burning in urban growth areas or large cities where reasonable alternatives exist, with narrow silvicultural exceptions; no ignition during air‑pollution episodes or impaired air quality; no prohibited materials; no fires within 50 feet of structures or 500 feet of forest slash without a written permit; and a capable person must attend the fire until completely extinguished. `[WAC 332-24-205]` - Certain small recreational‑type fires and small piles in specified counties and seasons do not require a written DNR permit but must meet size, equipment, and weather limits. `[WAC 332-24-211]` - Failure to comply with DNR burning rules voids permission to burn, constitutes a violation of `RCW 76.04.205` and chapter 70.94 RCW, may result in refusal of future permits for two years, and allows DNR to recover its response costs. `[WAC 332-24-217]` ### Effect of a district permit inside Seattle city limits - If the land inside Seattle is within a fire protection district that has properly adopted a burning‑permit resolution, the district may require and issue a written permit for open fires on that land, subject to its resolution and state law. `[RCW 52.12.101]` `[RCW 52.12.102]` - That district permit does not override state‑level prohibitions on outdoor burning in nonattainment areas, urban growth areas, or cities over 10,000 where alternatives exist; outdoor burning in those areas “shall not be allowed” except as specifically provided (e.g., storm/flood debris, certain agricultural or silvicultural burning). `[RCW 70A.15.5020]` `[RCW 70A.15.5010]` - A district permit also does not override DNR’s requirement for a DNR permit on lands under DNR protection, nor DNR’s authority to refuse, revoke, or suspend burning permits and privileges for safety or air‑quality reasons. `[RCW 76.04.205]` `[RCW 76.04.315]` `[WAC 332-24-201]` - If Ecology or the local air authority has delegated outdoor‑burning permit authority for a given burn type to the fire protection agency under `RCW 70A.15.5100` and `WAC 173-425-060`, then the district’s permit can serve as the clean‑air‑act permit, but it must be conditioned to protect air quality and comply with all applicable state requirements. `[RCW 70A.15.5030]` `[RCW 70A.15.5100]` `[WAC 173-425-060]` - If no such delegation exists, a district‑issued permit does not satisfy the requirement to obtain a permit from the air authority/Ecology (for non‑agricultural/silvicultural burning) or from Ecology/its delegate for agricultural burning; burning solely under a district permit in that case would violate the state outdoor‑burning program. `[RCW 70A.15.5090]` `[WAC 173-425-060]` - Regardless of any permit, if the fire is started or conducted contrary to chapter 52.12 RCW or permit conditions, the permittee commits a misdemeanor and may be liable for the district’s suppression costs. `[RCW 52.12.105]` `[RCW 52.12.106]` `[RCW 52.12.108]` ### Potential Conflicts & Risks | Issue | Agency A Requirement | Agency B Requirement | Tension | |--------------------------------------------|-----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|--------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------| | Burning in Seattle (urban growth area) | Fire district may issue permits within its district if it adopts a resolution. `[RCW 52.12.101]` | State law prohibits most outdoor burning in urban growth areas/large cities except narrow exceptions. `[RCW 70A.15.5020]` | A district permit cannot legalize burning that state clean‑air law prohibits; state statute prevails. | | Air‑quality episodes/impaired air quality | District may issue permits generally. `[RCW 52.12.101]` | No fire protection agency may issue permits during any stage of impaired air quality or air‑pollution episode. `[RCW 70A.15.5030]` | If a district issues a permit during an episode, the permit conflicts with state law and is ineffective. | | Lands under DNR protection in Seattle | District may issue permits on lands within its district (excluding forest/cut‑over land). `[RCW 52.12.101]` | DNR requires its own written permit for burning on lands it protects and may suspend all such permits. `[RCW 76.04.205]` `[RCW 76.04.315]` `[WAC 332-24-201]` | A district permit alone is insufficient on DNR‑protected lands; DNR authority under `RCW 76.04.205` controls. | | Agricultural burning | District can issue general burning permits under its resolution. `[RCW 52.12.101]` | Agricultural burning permits must come from Ecology/air authority or a delegated local entity. `[RCW 70A.15.5090]` | A district permit is valid for agricultural burning only if the district has been delegated that authority. | | Enforcement and penalties | Violating district permit rules is a misdemeanor and triggers cost recovery. `[RCW 52.12.105]` `[RCW 52.12.108]` | Violating DNR or clean‑air burning rules is a violation of `RCW 76.04.205` and chapter 70.94 RCW. `[WAC 332-24-217]` | A burner relying on an invalid/insufficient district permit may face both local misdemeanor and state penalties. | - No source provided addresses Seattle‑specific fire or land‑use ordinances on outdoor burning; only state‑level and generic municipal‑corporation provisions are in the materials.

**Vanilla vector RAG**: Correctness 4/5, Faithfulness 3/5

> If land is inside Seattle city limits, Seattle’s own codes and permits still control, even if another fire authority (like a fire protection district) issues a burning permit. Seattle’s Municipal Code makes clear that any authorization “in whatever form granted” to use City property or operate within the City does not substitute for other required City licenses or permits; separate City permits are still required under other City laws and regulations.[Source 9] SMC Title 22 establishes a Fire Code Permit and Fee Ordinance for activities regulated by the Seattle Fire Code, including hazardous processes, and authorizes the Seattle Fire Department (SFD) Fire Code Official to require temporary or other permits and to impose late fees or penalties when activities occur without required SFD permits.[Source 2][Source 5] Director’s Rule 24-2005 likewise assumes that fire department permits for hazardous materials and related activities are issued by the Seattle Fire Department’s Fire Marshal’s Office, and that SFD approval is a prerequisite for such regulated uses within Seattle.[Source 17] Side-by-Side Comparison | Topic | Agency | Requirement / Effect | Citation | |-------|--------|----------------------|----------| | Need for City permits despite other authorizations | SMC (Title 21 – Utilities) | Any authorization “in whatever form granted” to operate in a specified area of the City does not include any license or permit required to transact or carry on a business within the City under other City laws or regulations. | [Source 9] | | Fire code permits & fees | SMC (Title 22 – Fire Code Permit and Fee Ordinance) | Establishes that SFD charges fees for permits to store, handle, or use hazardous materials or conduct hazardous processes, and for related inspections and plan reviews, under its regulatory authority. | [Source 2] | | Operating without required SFD fire permits | SMC (Title 22 – Fire Code permits) | Any work activity or operation regulated by the Seattle Fire Code that commences before obtaining necessary permits must obtain a permit at 1.5 times the original fee; late filings are subject to late fees. | [Source 5] | | Fire department permits for hazardous materials/operations | DIR (Rule 24‑2005) | States that Fire Department permits are required to store, handle, or use flammable/combustible liquids and other hazardous materials, and directs applicants to the Seattle Fire Department Fire Marshal’s Office for permits and information. | [Source 17] | Friction Summary | # | Agencies | Friction Type | Winner | Risk Note | |---|----------|---------------|--------|-----------| | 1 | Outside fire protection district vs. SMC/SFD | Type IV — Preemption Risk (local jurisdictional control) | SMC/SFD (City authority within city limits) | A burning permit from a fire protection district does not satisfy Seattle’s own fire code permit requirements and does not authorize burning inside City limits. | Key Takeaways - A burning permit issued by a fire protection district does not replace or satisfy Seattle Fire Department permits for activities within Seattle city limits.[Source 9][Source 2] - Conducting a regulated burning activity in Seattle without required SFD permits can trigger higher permit fees (1.5×) and late penalties, even if you hold a non‑Seattle permit.[Source 5] - For any burning or hazardous fire-related activity on land inside Seattle, you must coordinate with and obtain permits from the Seattle Fire Department’s Fire Marshal’s Office, regardless of outside district approvals.[Source 17]

> In Seattle, a permit from a fire protection district does not override Seattle's jurisdiction or rules. ### Core rule Under the Seattle Fire Code and Washington State law, Seattle retains primary authority over fire prevention and outdoor burning within city limits. --- ### So what happens in your scenario? If a fire protection district issues a burning permit for land inside Seattle city limits: #### 1. The permit is not valid for burning in Seattle * Fire protection districts only have permitting authority within their own jurisdiction * Seattle is a separate fire authority * A district-issued permit does not authorize burning inside city limits --- #### 2. Seattle's prohibition still applies * Seattle generally prohibits outdoor burning * Any burning must comply with Seattle Fire Department rules—not external agencies --- #### 3. The fire protection district may have issued it in error Common reasons: * Incorrect jurisdiction mapping (property thought to be outside Seattle) * Annexation changes not reflected in permitting systems * Administrative oversight --- #### 4. Only Seattle Fire Department can authorize exceptions Even where limited burning is allowed (rare cases), it must be: * Permitted or approved by Seattle Fire Department * Compliant with Seattle's definitions of allowed recreational or special-use fires --- ### Bottom line * Fire district permit ≠ valid in Seattle * Seattle Fire Code controls entirely within city limits * If it's inside Seattle, only Seattle Fire Department authorization matters --- If you want, I can help you determine jurisdiction boundaries for a specific parcel—this issue comes up a lot near Seattle's edge where annexations and fire district lines overlap.
